[1] Chapter 1: Operation
[2] Detective Richard Quinn's personality had an unknown side that differed greatly from his energetic and practical approach to solving cases. Instead, he often enjoyed discussing his feelings at length from basic criminal concepts and theories. These professional speeches were usually delivered in front of the fireplace while reading the newspaper, with his detective partner and son, Ellery Quinn, as the audience. Besides the occasional ghostly gypsy boy, Juno, who brought necessary supplies, the room was empty.
[3] "The first five minutes after a crime are crucial," the old man said sternly. "Always remember that. You can save yourself a lot of trouble by making the most of these initial minutes."
[4] Ellery had been exposed to various investigative ideas since childhood. Hearing this statement, he looked into the fire, took a puff of his cigarette, and subconsciously complained, "How often does a detective encounter such good fortune as arriving at the crime scene within 300 seconds after the crime?"
[5] At home, he never concealed his doubts, and the old man could only nod sadly in agreement. After all, such luck was not always encountered. When investigators arrived at the crime scene, the traces had already disappeared, and you could only do your best to salvage a situation that was hopeless from the start.
[6] Ellery Quinn was not a fatalist or a determinist, but he was also not a pragmatist or a realist. The only thing he could associate with "ism" or "theory" was his firm trust and faith in human wisdom. This faith, like the great names and achievements in the history of thought, shone brilliantly. Of course, it was completely contradictory to his father's basic professional theories. Therefore, he greatly despised the conventional investigative procedures of the police, which he considered a humiliation of original thinking.
[7] This was the most concise summary of his philosophy of life. But in the case of Abigail Down, he almost gave up this philosophy of life. It may have been the first time in his entire unwavering intellectual career that he had encountered such intense self-doubt. This was not about his philosophy of life, as he had already validated it in previous cases. This doubt was more painful - it was whether he had the ability and wisdom to penetrate the secrets of another person's mind.
[8] In January of the 1920s, a cold, frosty morning, Ellery Quinn was strolling down the quiet East Sixty-fifth Street. He was wrapped tightly in a thick Ernst long coat, the brim of his soft felt hat low on his forehead, shielding his forehead and the glint of his spectacles. He was thinking about a case he was working on as he walked towards the next intersection and the row of buildings.
[9] This was indeed a frustrating case. Something must have happened between death and rigor mortis...
[10] He crossed the street, walking briskly towards the grandest building of all. In front of him, a wide, imposing marble spiral staircase rose from the sidewalk, merging at the top into a marble platform. On this platform stood a pair of massive iron doors with giant iron bolts, and above them was an inscription:
[11] He labored to climb the stairs and pushed open one of the heavy iron doors, entering the silent, high-ceilinged foyer. The floor was made of white marble, and the walls were covered with thick, dark enamel. To the left, a door stood open, with a white sign reading "Office," and to the right, another identical door bore the word "Waiting Room." At the back of the hall, behind a wobbling spring glass revolving door, he could just make out the elevator entrance, where an old man in a white coat sat.
[12] As Ellery stopped to look around, a stout, ruddy-faced man emerged from the office. He had a square jaw and wore a black fedora and a white lab coat.
[13] "Visiting hours are from 2:00 p.m. to 3:00 p.m.," he said gruffly. "Sir, I'm sorry, but you cannot see anyone until the designated time."
[14] "Eh?" Ellery shoved his hands deeper into his coat pockets. "I need to see Dr. Ming Chin as soon as possible!"
[15] "Oh?" Ellery shoved his hands deeper into his coat pockets. "I need to see Dr. Ming Chin as soon as possible!"
[16] The guard rubbed his chin. "Dr. Mingchin, is it? Did you make an appointment with him?"
[17] "Oh, he'll see me," Quine replied quickly. "Could you just find him for me? This is urgent and can't wait."
[18] "We don't accept tips here," the guard said regretfully. "I'll go and inform him. By the way, what's your name—"
[19] Ellery blinked and smiled, withdrawing the coin. "Ellery Queen. No tips, huh? What's your name? Cerberus [3]? "
[20] The guard looked perplexed at Quine. "No, sir. My name is Isaac Cooper, the 'special receptionist' here." He indicated the circular metal badge on his coat and walked away.
[21] Ellery entered the waiting room and sat down. The room was empty, and he couldn't help but furrow his brow, tapping the ground with his cane absentmindedly. A subtle scent of disinfectant stung his sensitive nose, making him uneasy.
[22] Soon, a tall, muscular man in a white coat burst into the room. "Ellery Queen! It's really you!” Ellery stood up, and they warmly shook hands. "What brings you here? Still on the case, as usual?"
[23] "Yes, the same old thing, John. It's a case," Ellery muttered. "I really don't like hospitals, and I always feel pressured when I come here. But I really need some information from you…"
[24] "Gladly," Dr. Ming replied eagerly. His bright blue eyes blinked a few times, and he smiled, placing his arm around Ellery's shoulder and leading him outside. "It's not a good place to talk here. Let's go to my office. We haven't seen each other in months, and we should catch up.”
[25] They walked through the glass revolving door, turned left, and entered a corridor. Bright light evenly illuminated the hallway, and the sides were lined with matching doors. All the doors were closed, and the smell of disinfectant grew stronger.
[26] "Oh, Zeus, the god of medicine!" Ellery exclaimed. "Doesn't this terrible smell bother you at all? Anyone else would have been finished by now.”
[27] Dr. Ming chuckled softly. They walked down the corridor to the end and turned right into a walkway perpendicular to the corridor. "You get used to it over time. After all, inhaling the foul-smelling phenol, mercury, and alcohol is better than inhaling the dangerous pathogenic bacteria all around us… How's the old detective lately?"
[28] "Same old, same old," Ellery said, frowning. "I'm handling a tricky little case – I've pretty much figured it out, but there's one detail left… If I'm right…"
[29] They turned right again and walked into the third corridor parallel to the first one they had just passed. The right side of the corridor was a solid white wall that extended to the end. There was a sturdy-looking door with the inscription "Operating Observation Hall" on it. On the left side of the corridor, they had just passed a door with the inscription "Dr. Lucius Tanning, Chief of Internal Medicine." A few steps ahead, another door had the inscription "Resting Room." Finally, Ellery's partner stopped at the third door, smiling. It read: "Dr. John Ming, Chief Medical Officer."
[30] The room was spacious and decorated simply. A large desk was the first thing to catch the eye. A few cabinets stood against the wall, and their glass shelves held gleaming metal medical instruments. There were four chairs, a low, wide bookshelf filled with thick books, and several metal filing cabinets.
[31] "Sit down, take off your coat, and feel free to speak your mind," Ming said. He sat down in the swivel chair behind the desk, leaned back, and let himself swing back and forth.
[32] "I have only one question," Ellery muttered. He quickly crossed the room, threw his coat over the chair, leaned against the desk, and leaned forward, his eyes fixed on Ming with intensity. "Do you know if, in certain cases, the time of rigor mortis can be different from what is normal?"
[33] "It's possible. What was the cause of death for this patient?"
[34] "Gunshot…"
[35] "Age?"
[36] "I estimate, around forty-five years old."
[37] "Has there been any unusual pathological phenomenon on his body? I mean...any symptoms? Like diabetes?"
[38] "I'm not sure about that."
[39] Mingqin gently rocked the chair. Ellery stepped back and sat down, reaching into his pocket for a cigarette.
[40] "I have some here...use mine," said Mingqin. "Well, Ellery, let me share my thoughts. Rigor mortis is a very complex phenomenon. In general, I always have to examine the body first before making a conclusion. I asked about diabetes because if a person is over forty years old and has high blood sugar levels, if they die from violence, they will inevitably enter the rigor mortis state within ten minutes...
[41] "Ten minutes? God!" Ellery stared at Ming, a half-smoked cigarette hanging from his mouth, "Ten minutes," he murmured to himself, "diabetes... John, can I borrow your phone?"
[42] "Sure." Ming pointed to the phone and sank deeper into the comfortable chair, content. Ellery dialed a number, spoke to two people, and then connected to the medical examiner's office. "Plauti? It's Ellery Q. I was checking Jimmy's body and found that he had sugar in his blood? What? Chronic diabetes, huh? Damn!"
[43] He slowly hung up the phone, took a deep breath, and flashed a smile. The lines of worry on his face instantly disappeared.
[44] "Thank you, John. This case is finally solved thanks to you. I'll make one more call and close it."
[45] He dialed the police station. "Inspector Q... Dad? The killer is Orluk... Yes. The broken leg... Yes, it was broken after death, but within ten minutes... Yes!... That's what I mean."
[46] "Don't rush off, Ellery," Mr. Ming rose, eagerly saying, "I'm free right now. You know, we haven't seen each other in years since the last time."
[47] They sat back down in their chairs, lighting cigarettes. Ellery's face showed a calm, resolute expression.
[48] "My dear friend, I can stay here all day if you need me." He chuckled. "You just provided the final straw that broke the camel's back, and I can't thank you enough... But, you know, I don't need to be so hard on myself. Without systematically studying such fascinating medical knowledge, I wouldn't have known anything about diabetes."
[49] "That's right, so we're not completely useless in our profession." Mr. Ming said. "In fact, the word 'diabetes' has been in my mind all morning. The most prominent figure in this hospital happens to have chronic diabetes, and she had an unfortunate accident this morning. She slipped and fell from the top step of the stairs to the bottom. Her gallbladder burst, and the injuries were severe. Dr. Jeni is preparing for emergency surgery."
[50] "I'm sorry. Who is this unfortunate person?"
[51] "Abigail Dunn," Ming said solemnly. "She's over seventy years old. Despite her excellent health, a gallbladder surgery like this is a headache with diabetes as a factor. However, there's a good point too, which is that she's still unconscious and doesn't need anesthesia for the surgery. We planned to perform surgery on her next month for a mild chronic appendicitis. But now, Dr. Jenkins won't touch her appendix today, or her condition may become more complicated. However, if the patient wasn't Mrs. Dunn, Dr. Jenkins wouldn't have to worry about this, as this is a minor surgery for him. He looked at his watch, "The surgery will start at 10:45 a.m. – it's already 10:00 a.m. – would you like to stay and watch Dr. Jenkins' exquisite surgical skills?"
[52] "Uh..."
[53] "He's a miracle, you know that?" Ming said. "He's the best surgeon on the East Coast and the chief surgeon at the Dutch Memorial Hospital. This is partly due to his personal friendship with Mrs. Dunn. Of course, his top-notch surgical skills also make him well-suited for the job. Mrs. Dunn has been very good to him and appreciates his talents, so she has kept him in this hospital. Dr. Jenkins will perform the surgery in the main operating room across the hall. He will undoubtedly help Mrs. Dunn overcome this ordeal. He promised me that, you can trust him."
[54] "Okay, you've convinced me, I want to stay and watch." Eliot said dejectedly, "I've never attended a surgical operation before. Do you think I can handle it? I mean, will I faint or something? You know I get sick easily, really, John..."
[55] "This shocks us all," Dr. Ming said, stretching his arms thoughtfully. "Yes, Abigail Dunn... I guess you know she founded the Dutch Memorial Hospital, Eliot? Her blood, her money – it's her hospital... We were all shocked when we heard about this. Dr. Jenkins is more concerned about Mrs. Dunn's condition than anyone else – she's like his godmother – she funded his education at Johns Hopkins University Medical School, then sent him to Vienna, and then to the University of Paris – that's how Dr. Jenkins became the doctor he is today. So it's only natural that he insisted on surgical treatment and insisted on performing the surgery himself. No one is more suitable for this than him."
[56] "How did the accident happen?" Ellery inquired curiously.
[57] "This is fate, I guess... you know, every Monday morning, she would come to the hospital to inspect the charity ward (it was her idea to establish this charity ward). When she was walking down the stairs from the fourth floor to the third floor, she suddenly had an episode, and her diabetes caused her to become unconscious... she fell down the stairs, landing on her stomach... Fortunately, Jenny was nearby at the time. He immediately checked her injuries and noticed from the outside that her gallbladder had ruptured - her entire abdomen was severely swollen... There was no choice, so Jenny had to give her an emergency injection of insulin glucose."
[58] "What is the cause of this coma?"
[59] "We have found out that this is due to the negligence of Sarah Fowler, who is a middle-aged woman who has been caring for Abigail Dunn for some time. She has been helping to manage household affairs and accompany Mrs. Dunn. Mrs. Dunn's condition requires three injections of insulin glucose per day to maintain stability. Dr. Jennings has always insisted on administering the injections himself. Injecting insulin is not a complicated matter, and under normal circumstances, the patient can perform the injection without any major issues. However, last night, Dr. Jennings had an emergency surgery to attend to, which caused this to be delayed. As usual, if he couldn't go to the Dunn home that night, he would call Abigail's daughter, Hilda, to remind her. Unfortunately, on that particular evening, Hilda was not at home, and Fowler answered the phone. He left a message for Fowler to remind Hilda to give her mother the insulin injection when she returned home, but Fowler forgot about it entirely. Abigail herself was not very diligent about the injections, so no injection was given last night. Hilda was unaware of Dr. Jennings' call and woke up late this morning. As a result, Abigail did not receive an injection again this morning. To make matters worse, Abigail had a hearty breakfast this morning, which proved to be fatal. Her blood sugar levels skyrocketed, leading to her fainting. Additionally, she was unlucky enough to fall from the top of the stairs. If she had fallen halfway down, the situation would not have been as dire. Now, we are in this predicament."
[60] "This is such a sad story!" Ellery murmured, "Did all the relevant parties get notified? I bet there will be a sweet family gathering at the hospital soon."
[61] "They can't have a gathering in the operating room, they can't do that," Dr. Ming said sternly. "They'll be waiting in the waiting room next door. Family members are not allowed to enter the observation gallery during surgery. Do you know that rule? Anyway, it doesn't matter. Would you like to take a look around? I'd be happy to show you around the hospital. I must say, this hospital is a model in its field."
[62] "I'd love to, John."
[63] They left Dr. Ming's office and walked down the north corridor they had just passed. Dr. Ming pointed to the door next to the observation gallery they would be observing the surgery in, which was the door to the restroom, and whispered, "Some members of the Dowen family may have already arrived." He commented, "We won't let them wander around... There are two auxiliary operating rooms on the west corridor... We are always fully booked, busy to death - we are one of the largest surgical teams in the east... On the left side of the corridor opposite, is the main operating room, which we call the Roman Colosseum. The main operating room also includes two special rooms, one is the surgical preparation room, and the other is the anesthesia room. Look, this door at the end of the corridor leads to the surgical preparation room, and the other one - the one to the west - leads to the anesthesia room. The anesthesia room has another entrance on the south corridor... All major surgeries are performed in the main operating room. Some demonstrative, educational surgeries are also performed here to show the interns and nurses in the observation gallery. Of course, we have several more operating rooms upstairs."
[64] The hospital was shrouded in a strange silence. Occasionally, a white figure would silently pass through the long hall. All sounds within the building seemed to have been eliminated; every door hinge was coated with a thick layer of lubricant, so the opening and closing of doors would not cause any noise. Soft light filled all the spaces inside the building, and the air seemed extremely pure, except for a slight chemical smell.

[66] "That's a good question," Mingo commended. "Indeed, in most cases – almost all cases – anesthesia is necessary. You know – I assume you don't – that any surgical procedure is quite dangerous for patients with chronic diabetes. Even the smallest surgery can be fatal. Let me tell you a case – just a few days ago, a patient with gangrene in his toe was admitted to the hospital – poor guy. The on-call physician – well, it was just an unpredictable medical incident in daily practice. After cleaning the wound on the toe, the patient was sent home. But the next morning, he was dead. The autopsy showed that the deceased had extremely high blood sugar levels, but it's likely that even the deceased himself didn't know about it...
[67] "What I just talked about means that any wound can be fatal for patients with diabetes. If surgery is inevitable, we must take preventive measures beforehand – we must restore the patient's blood sugar levels to normal as quickly as possible. Even during the surgery, we need to cross-inject insulin and glucose into the patient and repeatedly check the patient's blood sugar levels. They must also use this method to deal with Mrs. Dougherty's condition. She was injected with insulin glucose and her blood sugar level was checked. These emergency preventive measures will take about one to two hours. In non-emergency situations, this would take a whole month, as it would harm the liver. But for Mrs. Dougherty, we have no choice. A ruptured gallbladder is no small matter, and we can't even wait half a day."

[100] She nestled into his embrace, grasping his hand. After a while, they walked to a nearby bench and sat down. Hilda Dauwen's beautiful, vacant eyes stared at the floor. The young man found himself wanting to comfort the girl but didn't know what to say, so he just held her silently.
[101] Silence enveloped them. Although the hospital was bustling with activity, this room was eerily quiet. There were no footsteps, no cheers, no sound at all, only the dull white walls standing there silently...
[102] "Oh, Phil, I'm so scared, so scared!"

[105] Chapter Three: A Visit
[106] A short, peculiar-looking man appeared at the end of the corridor and made his way towards Mignon and Ellery. Although his features were not clearly visible, Ellery had already imprinted his physical appearance in his mind. This impression deepened as he observed the man's stiff movements. His head was held unnaturally, and his left leg seemed to have some issue, causing him to limp as he walked, always placing his weight on his right leg. "Seems like some kind of muscle paralysis," Ellery muttered under his breath, as the doctor approached them.
[107] The man's entire attire indicated that he had just completed an operation - wearing a surgical gown, white overalls, and white canvas shoes; the gown bore traces of chemical stains, and one sleeve's hem trailed a long bloodstain. He walked over, fumbling with the band on his head, attempting to remove his mask.
[108] "Ah, Mignon doctor! Done, it's an appendix perforation, under control, won't develop into peritonitis. Dirty work, hard work... How's Abigail? Have you seen her? What was her last blood sugar level? And who's this?" His speech was as fast as a machine gun, his bright eyes darting back and forth between Mignon and Ellery.
[109] "Dr. Jenning, meet Mr. Quincy. An old friend of mine," Mignon replied. "The writer, Ellery Quincy."
[110] "Pleasure to meet you, doctor," Ellery said. "I'm delighted to make your acquaintance."

Luckily, her heart function is still good. The gallbladder burst, a bit difficult. How about the intravenous injection?"
[112] "Everything is going smoothly," Doctor Ming Jin answered. "The last report I received was around ten o'clock, and they have already reduced it from 180 to 135. Everything must be strictly according to the schedule. She should be in the surgical preparation room by now."
[113] "Great! She'll be able to walk around freely soon."
[114] Ellery smiled apologetically and asked, "Gentlemen, please forgive my ignorance, but what did you mean by the mysterious 180 and 135 in your previous conversation? Is it blood pressure?"
[115] "My God, of course not!" Dr. Jenny exclaimed. "180 milligrams of blood sugar in 180 cubic centimeters of blood. We need to lower the blood sugar level to a normal level before performing the surgery - about 101 to 102. Oh, you're not a healthcare professional, sorry."
[116] "I learned a lot," Ellery said.

A revolutionary work in medical history. And I found the most exceptional co-author in our field. Quin, do you know what 'congenital sensitivity' means? I guess you don't. This title will cause a stir in the medical community. We've also proven some things in the fracture field that have been unresolved in the medical community for decades…"

[120] "Sorry," Dr. Jeni suddenly turned around, "Cooper. What do you want?"
[121] It turned out that there was a white-clad doorkeeper behind them, hesitating whether to approach and interrupt the conversation of the three. He took off his hat.
[122] "Dr. Jenkins, there's someone at the door who wants to see you," he said hastily. "He said he made an appointment with you beforehand. I apologize for disturbing you, doctor —"
[123] "He's a fraud," Dr. Jenkins roared. "Cooper, you know I can't see anyone right now. I've told you so many times not to bother me with this. Where's Miss Price? You know she'll take care of these things for me. Go away, we'll continue. Damn it, I can't see this person. I'm busy."
[124] He turned his back on the doorkeeper. Cooper's face turned even redder, but he still didn't leave.
[125] "But — she — he said…"
[126] "You must have forgotten, doctor," Mr. Mingjing interjected. "Miss Price has been copying the manuscript of 'Congenital Sensitivity' all morning, and now she has gone to take care of Mrs. D'Orne according to your instructions..."
[127] "Well, damn it!" Dr. Jenning muttered. "Then I won't see anyone, Cooper, I -"
[128] The doorkeeper remained silent, extending his large hand and presenting a business card to the surgeon, as if presenting a precious document. Dr. Jenning took the business card. "Who is it? Schwarz - Schwarz... Oh!"
[129] His voice instantly changed, and the gloom fell over his bright eyes. He then neatly tucked the business card into his coat pocket with a precise movement, quickly took out his watch from his pants pocket and looked at it. "Ten twenty-nine," he murmured. Erlie was amazed at his agile hand movements - he smoothly put the watch back in his pocket and smoothed out the wrinkles on his white coat. "Well, Cooper!" he said. "Lead the way. Where is he?... See you later, John. Goodbye, Quinn."

[134] "Almost… He's not just eccentric, Erlie. He's a doctor who has dedicated his entire life to the medical profession. He has given his body and his fortune to the advancement of medicine. As far as I know, he has never refused a diagnosis due to financial reasons. In fact, he has even treated people for free several times… Don't misunderstand him, Erlie. He's a truly remarkable man."

[137] They entered Dr. Ming Jin's office. Dr. Ming Jin made a phone call, asking a few questions and seemed satisfied with the answers.
[138] "They've sent Abby to the prep room," he said, putting down the phone. "They've brought her blood sugar down to 110 milligrams - everything is ready, but we can only truly be at ease when the surgery is over."
[139] Ellery shuddered slightly. Dr. Ming Jin pretended not to notice. The two of them lit cigarettes in silence, puffing away, a subtle sense of unease filling the air.
[140] Ellery shrugged, as if trying to shake something off. He exhaled another puff of smoke. "Speaking of collaborations, John," he said cheerfully, "I never would have thought you'd pick up a writing habit. What are you writing?"
[141] "Ah, that," Doctor Ming laughed. "Most of the book is based on historical case analysis. Jenny and I proposed a theory, and after analyzing a large number of cases, we believe that certain innate factors within the human body can cause abnormal bodily functions, and studying these factors can predict the diseases a patient will develop in the future. Sounds complicated, doesn't it?"
[142] "Too academic, too professional, Professor," Quine grumbled. "Mind if I take a look at the manuscript? Maybe I can help you with the wording."
[143] Doctor Ming's face flushed. "Sorry, no can do, old friend," he said with a slight awkwardness in his voice. "Jenny would kill me. In fact, the manuscript and all the cases are top secret for us. Jenny protects them like her own life. Why? Just recently, the old man hired a doctor who was so curious that he actually sneaked a peek at Jenny's safe – purely out of academic curiosity... Sorry, Ellery. Only Jenny, me, and Miss Price, Jenny's assistant, a professional nurse – we're only responsible for the clerical work."
[144] "Okay, okay!" Ellery closed his eyes and smiled. "I surrender. I just wanted to help. Don't you remember the story of The Iliad? 'Many hands make light work.' Since you've declined my offer...”

[146] Chapter 4: Revealing
[147] Ellery Quine is a renowned amateur criminologist, but he is also a hemophobe. His mind is filled with stories of crime and murder from a young age, and as he grew older, he spent countless hours dealing with police and criminals. However, the sight of blood and gore still makes him feel uncomfortable at his core. As the son of a police officer, he often grapples with the most ruthless and evil minds, making him quite knowledgeable about criminal psychology - both as a writer specializing in the field and from a societal perspective. However, in reality, he still struggles to remain composed in the face of the gruesome scenes left behind by human violence. Each time he visits a crime scene, he is sharp-eyed, accurate in his reasoning, and quick in his judgment, but he always feels nauseous...
[148] He had never attended a surgical procedure. Although he had seen many corpses, in the morgue, unrecognizable due to being dissected, retrieved from rivers or seas, flattened on train tracks, or abandoned on the streets after gang fights - he had a rich, deep, and bitter understanding of these ugly ways of death. But the thought of cold metal cutting through warm flesh, exposing living tissue, severing blood vessels, and touching fresh red blood - these images would make him nauseous.
[149] He was now sitting in the observation gallery of the Dutch Memorial Hospital's operating room, his heart racing with both fear and excitement. His eyes were fixed on the proceedings taking place twenty feet away in the operating room. The scene before him was calm, busy, and well-organized. Mingjing sat beside him, slouching in his chair, his blue eyes taking in all the preparations before the surgery. He could hear whispers from the other observers in the observation gallery. In the center of the observation gallery, a group of men and women in white coats sat - they were the hospital's interns and nurses, observing the professional surgical techniques. They were very quiet. Behind Ellery and Mingjing sat a man in a hospital uniform, and a girl who looked quite frail. The girl was whispering something in the man's ear. This man was the chief of internal medicine, Dr. Lucas Tanning, and the girl was his daughter, who worked at a social welfare institution. Dr. Tanning had gray hair, wrinkles on his face, and his brown eyes looked kindly at the front. The girl looked average, not particularly pretty, and her left eyelid kept twitching.
[150] The floor of the observation gallery was raised, separated from the operating room by an unscalable white barrier. The layout was similar to a lecture hall, with rows of seats that got progressively higher - similar to the boxes in a theater. The highest wall had a door, and a spiral staircase outside led to the north corridor.
[151] There was a sound of footsteps outside, and the door suddenly opened, revealing Philip Muhous, who rushed in, his eyes darting around the room. His brown coat and hat were nowhere to be seen. He spotted Dr. Ming from a distance and hurried down the stairs to speak to him in a low voice.
[152] Dr. Ming nodded solemnly and turned to Ellery, introducing him to Philip Muhous. "This is Mr. Muhous, the lawyer for Mrs. Dorn," he said, gesturing to the two of them. They shook hands, and Ellery forced a smile before turning back to the front of the gallery.
[153] Philip Muhous was a tall, slender man with a firm gaze and a prominent jawline. "Hilda, Fowler, and Hendrik Dorn – they're downstairs in the lounge. Can they be present during the surgery?" he asked urgently. Dr. Ming shook his head and gestured for him to sit down. Muhous frowned but complied. He was immediately captivated by the frantic activity of the medical staff.
[154] An elderly man in a white coat appeared at the top of the stairs, exchanging a nod with an intern before disappearing again. The door clicked shut, and the sound echoed throughout the silent main operating theater, signifying the start of the performance. The sound of the man's footsteps faded away.
[155] Everyone in the observation gallery held their breath, waiting in silence. Ellery felt the tension in the air akin to the stillness before the curtain rises in a theater, with the audience holding their breath and the theater silent. Under the bright, even light of three surgical lamps, a bare operating table stood still, devoid of any color. Nearby, a small table was set up with bandages, cotton, and small bottles and jars containing various medications. A glass-covered box held a collection of gleaming metallic surgical instruments.
[156] "First, mercuric chloride, then alcohol," Dr. Ming whispered to Ellery.
[157] Once the alcohol had evaporated from his hands, the assistant surgeon immediately stretched out his arms, holding them parallel. A nurse retrieved a pair of rubber gloves from the sterilizer and put them on his hands. The same scene played out with the other assistant surgeon.
[158] Suddenly, the door on the left side of the hall opened, and Dr. Jeny limped into the operating room. He looked around the room, then quickly hobbled over to the sink. He quickly removed his surgical gown, and a nurse swiftly dressed him in a freshly sterilized surgical gown. As he bent over to wash his hands with blue mercuric chloride solution, another nurse placed a new white surgical cap on his head and carefully tucked his gray hair back inside.
[159] Dr. Jeny did not look up, issuing a command. "Patient." Two assistant nurses opened the door to the surgical preparation room. "Patient, Miss Price!" one of them called out. They disappeared from the main operating room, returning shortly with a long, four-wheeled gurney bearing a still patient covered in a white sheet. The patient's head was deeply tilted back, eyes closed, and face pale. The sheet covered the neck. A third nurse entered the main operating room from the surgical preparation room and quietly stood by, waiting for instructions.
[160] The patient was lifted off the gurney and placed on the operating table. The gurney was taken by a third nurse, who pushed it back into the surgical preparation room. She quietly closed the door and disappeared from view. Near the operating table, a doctor in a white surgical suit and mask was bent over, arranging small instruments and equipment.
[161] "That's the anesthesiologist," Mingqin whispered, "He's always ready in case Abby wakes up during the surgery."
[162] The two assistant surgeons approached the operating table from opposite directions. They lifted the white sheet covering the patient and immediately covered it with a surgical gown of a peculiar cut. Dr. Jenny, who had already put on gloves, a surgical gown, and a surgical cap, patiently waited to one side. A nurse was putting a surgical mask over his mouth and nose.
[163] Mingqin leaned forward, with a strange feeling in his eyes. He stared at the patient's body and whispered something to Ellery, his tone changing.
[164] "Something's wrong, Ellery, something's wrong!"
[165] Ellery didn't turn around and answered him. "Is it rigor mortis?" He whispered, "I noticed it. A diabetic patient..."
[166] The two resident doctors bent over the operating table, checking the situation. One lifted the patient's arm and then let it fall naturally. But the arm was stiff and couldn't bend. The other checked the patient's eyelids and observed the eyeballs. They looked at each other in astonishment.
[167] "Dr. Jenny!" one of them exclaimed in terror.
[168] Dr. Jenny turned around and widened his eyes. "What's going on?" he pushed the nurse aside and hobbled over to the patient. He pulled off the drape, felt the patient's neck. Ellery saw his back shudder, as if he had been hit by a hammer.
[169] Without raising his head, Dr. Jenny's mouth spat out two words: "Epinephrine! Ventilator!" The two words seemed like magic spells. Hearing them, the two resident doctors, two nurses, and two other nurses immediately got to work. One nurse handed Dr. Jenny a small flashing object. He pried open the patient's mouth, inserted the metal object into her mouth, and examined its surface – it was a mirror. He cursed, threw the mirror aside, and took the injection syringe already prepared from another nurse's hand. He ripped open the patient's chest drape and injected it directly into the heart. The ventilator began to work, continuously supplying oxygen to the patient's lungs…
[170] The nurses, interns, Tanning doctor and daughter, Philip Mauhaus, Mingqin doctor, and Ellery leaned forward, motionless. The entire observation hall was silent except for the sound of the automatic ventilator.
[171] Fifteen minutes later, at 11:05 - Elly subconsciously glanced at his watch - Dr. Jeni, who had been crouching over the patient, stood up straight and left the patient. He angrily gestured at Dr. Ming. Dr. Ming silently left his seat, ran down the spiral staircase door, and disappeared at the door. A little while later, he rushed out of the observation hall door and ran to the main operating room. Dr. Jeni stepped back and pointed at the old woman's neck.
[172] Dr. Ming's face turned white as a sheet... like Dr. Jeni, he also subconsciously took a step back. This time, he gestured at Elly, who had been motionless for a long time in the observation hall, as if he had turned to stone.
[173] Elly stood up. His eyebrows arched, and his lips formed a word silently. Dr. Ming saw this word.
[174] Dr. Ming nodded.
[175] The word was -
[176] "Murder?"
[177] Chapter 5: Strangulation

[483][1] The Semites, also known as the Semitic people, are a group of ethnic groups originating from the Arabian Peninsula. The descendants of Noah's son Shem are believed to be their ancestors. Arabs and Jews are both Semitic people. The majority of the population in the Middle East and North Africa are descendants of ancient Semitic people who have been Arabized.
[484][2] Henry Wadsworth Longfellow (1807-1882) was an American poet and translator.
[485] Chapter Nine: Entanglements
[486] "So," after the three policemen closed the door, Ellery bowed respectfully to Dr. Jennings, "let's trace back to the source of knowledge, Doctor… who is this mysterious visitor of yours?"
[487] Inspector Quin got up. He walked lightly around the room, careful not to break any spells or curses. Ellery stood still – even the practical, unimaginative police in the room could see the dramatic elements in his light-hearted question.
[488] Dr. Jennings didn't answer immediately. He bit his lips and furrowed his brow, as if arguing with himself over a complex, unsolvable problem. When he finally spoke, his eyebrows relaxed into a peaceful shape.
[489] He said succinctly, "Quin, don't make a big deal out of it. My guest is an old friend…"
[490] "A friend named Schwarz,"
[491] "Yes. He's been having some financial difficulties lately, so he came to me to borrow some money."
[492] "That's not a bad thing, is it?" Ellery muttered, "He needs money, so he comes to you to borrow some... there's nothing to hide, right?" He smiled, "Of course, you must have lent him some?"
[493] The surgeon stiffened. "Yes - I gave him a personal check for fifty dollars."
[494] Ellery laughed, as if he had asked a question he already knew the answer to. "You don't have to worry, doctor! It's not a big deal... by the way, what's the name of your friend?"
[495] He paused nonchalantly, as if the question he posed was the most natural thing in the world. Inspector Queene's eyes fixed on Dr. Jenning, his hand rummaging in his pocket for a long time, finally pulling out an old brown snuff jar. He held it between his hand and his nose, waiting quietly.
[496] Jenny's response was direct and brief. "I don't want to answer that!" Detective Quine's hand continued with its motion, returning to its original position. He took a deep breath of snuff, stood up, and took a step forward, his gentle face showing a hint of curiosity.
[497] However, Ellery stepped in. He spoke calmly: "And that's exactly what I want to know, doctor. Since you're so eager to defend Mr. Schwarz, he must be an important person to you. A good friend, perhaps?"
[498] "Oh, no!" Jenny answered quickly.
[499] "Not?" Ellery's eyebrows raised. "From your actions and attitude, Doctor Quine…" He stepped forward, looking down at the small doctor. "You just need to answer one question, doctor, and then I'll stop.”
[500] "I don't know what you're getting at," Doctor Quine murmured, taking a step back.
[501] "Whatever I ask,” Ellery said gently, "you only need to answer. If this person named Schwarz is not a particularly close friend of yours, why did you take fifteen precious minutes out of your time this morning, when your female patient was in a diabetic coma, her condition was serious, and she was lying there waiting for your life-saving treatment, just to see him?"
[502] Doctor Jeni's eyes glowed with defiant resistance, and he coldly said, "I can't say anything related to this case investigation."
[503] Ellery turned away and slowly walked to the chair where his father had just sat, waving his hand as if to say, "This person is yours."
[504] The old man smiled and became even more gentle. He walked back and forth in the room. Doctor Jeni's provocative little eyes watched him closely.
[505] "There's no need to say more, Doctor Jeni," the old officer said politely. "We cannot accept your attitude and stance on this issue. I think you should also be clear about this…" It seemed he intended to appeal to his emotions, but this was a challenge. "Perhaps you can respect me and give me face by answering me directly. We don't need to play these games anymore."
[506] Jenny said nothing.
[507] "Okay, let's start... I'll start with the questions. You spent fifteen minutes with this person named Schwarz in your office. What happened during that time?"
[508] "I'm not trying to give you trouble," Dr. Jenny's attitude changed dramatically, he leaned tiredly against the back of the chair, relying on the chair to support himself, "I've already said that Schwarz came to me because he wanted to borrow fifty dollars. He needed it urgently and couldn't borrow it from anywhere else for the time being. At first, I refused, and he explained his current predicament to me. His request was reasonable, and his words were sincere, making it impossible for me to refuse. Then I gave him a check, we talked about his current situation, and he left."
[509] "Your explanation is reasonable, Dr. Jenny," the policeman said sternly, "but if the situation is as you say, then why do you hide the name and address of this person? You must know that we have our own procedures and means to continue investigating this matter, and the testimony of your friend is necessary for our investigation. Tell us what we need to know, and that's it."
[510] Jenny shook her head vigorously. "I'm sorry, officer... I can only explain as follows: my friend is an innocent bystander who has been dragged into the current situation - he comes from a good background, has a pure nature, but any exposure and infamy would be devastating for him. Moreover, he has nothing to do with the murder of Mrs. Dawn. His voice became more shrill, "God, why do you have to keep pursuing him?"
[511] Ellery thoughtfully polished his glasses, his gaze never leaving Jenny's face.
[512] "I suppose it's impossible for you to describe Swanson for us?" the officer asked, the smile fading from his face.
[513] Jenny clamped his lips shut.
[514] "Well, then," the old officer blurted out, "you must realize that without Swanson's testimony, your situation would also be quite precarious. So, what do you say, Doctor Jenny?"
[515] "I have nothing to say."
[516] "I'll give you one more chance, Dr. Jenning," the officer's cold tone held a great deal of anger, his lips trembling slightly, "give me Svenson's card."
[517] A suffocating silence fell over the scene.
[518] "What?" Jenning exclaimed.
[519] "The card! Hand it over!" the old officer impatiently shouted, "Give me the card with Svenson's name on it! The one the gatekeeper gave you when you were talking to Dr. Ming-Jin and Mr. Quin. Where is it?"
[520] Jenning's eyes were weary as he looked at the old officer helplessly, "It's not on me."
[521] "Where is it?"
[522] Jenning remained motionless in his seat.
[523] The elderly officer turned to the angry-looking Willy, standing in the corner, and shouted, "Search him!"
[524] The surgeon drew in a sharp breath and retreated to the wall, looking at Willy in terror like a hunted animal. Ellery rose from his chair but quickly sat back down. Willy backed the little doctor into a corner and said coldly, "Do it yourself or I'll take it from you."
[525] "For God's sake!" Jenning exclaimed, his face turning pale with anger, "If you touch me, I'll—" His voice trailed off as he realized there was no point in resisting.
[526] Willis grabbed the frail doctor with his massive arm, handling him as easily as a small infant. Despite trembling violently, the surgeon gave up resistance, and his anger faded as his eyes glazed over and stared straight ahead.
[527] "No," Willis stepped back and returned to the corner.
[528] Detective Quin stared solemnly at the little doctor, a hint of admiration growing in his heart. He gave no indication of turning around and issued the cold command, "Search Jani's office, Thomas."
[529] Willis left the room with a police officer, the sound of their footsteps echoing.
[530] Ellery was crestfallen. He quietly stood up and whispered something to the officer, who shook his head in confusion.
[531] "Dr. Jeni." Ellery's voice was low and deep. The surgeon leaned weakly against the wall, his eyes fixed on the floor. His face was flushed, and he was panting. His pants were wrinkled. "Dr. Jeni, I'm sorry for what happened. But it was you who left us with no choice... we tried - really tried to put ourselves in your shoes... Doctor, if this Mr. Schwarz was really your friend and knew you were covering for him so desperately, I think he would have been very eager to come and confirm everything you said. Doctor, am I not right? No matter how unfortunate his situation is now..."
[532] "I'm sorry..." Jeni's voice was already very hoarse, and Ellery had to lean forward to listen. The resistance in him had disappeared, leaving only endless fatigue.
[533] "I see." Ellery said seriously, "I have one last question, and it seems I have no way to force you to answer... Dr. Jeni, from the time you entered the office until the time you parted, did you or Mr. Schwarz ever leave the office, even for a moment?"
[534] "No." Dr. Jeni looked up and met Ellery's eyes.
[535] "Thank you." Ellery sat back down. He took out a cigarette, lit it, and took a thoughtful puff.
[536] Inspector Quinlan gave a brief instruction to a nearby detective, who promptly disappeared. Soon after, he returned with Isaac Cooper, who strode confidently into the room, his face aglow with energy.
[537] "Cooper," the inspector began, getting straight to the point, "you said you saw Mr. Schwartz when he arrived and left the hospital. Describe his appearance and demeanor."
[538] "Oh, certainly!" Cooper's face lit up with a smile. "I never forget a face, sir... yes, sir. He was of medium height, a fair-haired gentleman with blue eyes, clean-shaven chin, dressed in dark clothing, and wearing a black coat."
[539] "Can you tell," Ellery asked immediately, "if this man was wealthy?—I mean, judging by his attire and such?"
[540] "Good heavens, no!" Cooper shook his head sympathetically. "Considering his shoe heels, I would say he was in quite a predicament... yes, certainly—oh, and he was probably around thirty-five or forty-five years old."
[541] "How long have you been working here, Cooper?" Ellery asked.
[542] "Nine years and a few months, almost ten."
[543] Officer Quinn asked coldly, "Have you ever seen this man named Schwarz, Cooper?"
[544] The doorman didn't answer immediately. "Er...uh, he seems a bit familiar... but I can't remember."
[545] "Hmm!" Officer Quinn took a pinch of snuff. "Cooper," he breathed heavily as he inhaled the snuff, "you should remember his last name! You gave him a business card to Dr. Jenning." He quickly added, tossing the snuffbox back into his pocket, "It was you who gave him the card."
[546] The doorman looked terrified. "What? I...I didn't even look at it! I just gave it to Dr. Jeni."
[547] "Cooper, my dear friend," Eleri interjected lazily, "this is quite surprising! You refuse to accept a tip, and you don't even feel curious about who gave you the tip. That's strange!"
[548] "Are you telling me," the officer asked threateningly, "that after that person gave you the business card, you walked the entire length of the corridor to find Dr. Jeni without even looking at that card once?"
[549] "I...no, sir. I didn't," Cooper was truly frightened.
[550] "Idiot!" the old detective grumbled, turning away from him. "This guy is an idiot. Get out, Cooper!"
[551] Cooper left silently. As Cooper was being questioned, Chief Willis had quietly entered the room. He now approached the officer silently.
[552] "How's it going, Thomas?" It was clear that the old officer had no hope for Chief Willis's report. He glared at Willis angrily. Ellery glanced at Dr. Jeni, who seemed indifferent to the matter and was lost in thought.
[553] "No business card found."
[554] "Ha!" The officer slowly approached Dr. Jeni. "Where did you put that business card? Answer me!" He was livid.
[555] Dr. Jeni was exhausted. "I burned it," he said.
[556] "Good," the officer roared, "Thomas!"
[557] "Yes!"
[558] "Get everyone mobilized. I want this guy named Schwarz to be in the station tonight. Medium build, blonde hair, dark clothes, sloppy dress, about 35 years old, poor financial status. Go, start the operation!"
[559] Ellery sighed and said, "Willis. The officer stopped at the door. "Wait a minute..." Ellery turned to face Dr. Jennings, "Doctor, would you mind if I take a look at your checkbook?"
[560] Jennings spasmed with anger, and anger shot out of his eyes again. But when he spoke, his voice was still full of endless fatigue. "Be my guest." He reached into his back pocket and pulled out a folded checkbook, handing it silently to Ellery.
[561] Ellery quickly flipped through the checkbook from the first page to the last page. On the torn-off stub at the end, there was a note written in cash.
[562] "Ah!" Ellery smiled and handed the checkbook back to Dr. Jeni. Dr. Jeni put it back in his pocket expressionlessly.
[563] "Willi, find that check. Your next stop should be the Dutch Bank, followed by the Clearing House. The check number is 1886, for fifty dollars in cash, dated today, from Dr. Jeni's private account. Regardless, as soon as you find that check, you'll get Swanson's signature.
[564] "One more thing!" Ellery's voice suddenly rang out, "Did you notice the name 'Swanson' in Dr. Jeni's personal address book when you searched his office?"
[565] Willi's lips curled into a cold smile. "Of course, we checked, but we found nothing. There was no one with that name. There was no name 'Swanson' in the phone book under the doctor's desk either. Anything else?"
[566] "No."
[567] "Hey," the policeman approached Dr. Jenning, "you don't have to stand like that, doctor," he said in a friendly tone, "why don't you sit down?" The surgeon looked at him in surprise and dullness. "After all," the old man said coldly, "we'll be here for a while..."
[568] Dr. Jenning slumped back into the chair. The room fell silent until there was a knock on the door leading to the west corridor, and a detective entered.
[569] Detective Little appeared in the hallway, holding a large, shapeless package of white under his arm. Two other detectives, Johnson and Hesse, followed him, looking more composed. They grinned foolishly.
[570] Inspector Quin leaned forward. Ellery stood up and took a step forward. Dr. Jenning's head drooped almost to his stomach, looking like he was sleeping.
[571] "What is this?" the old inspector shouted, grabbing the white package.
[572] "It's clothes, boss," Little yelled, "we found the killer's clothes!"
[573] The old inspector placed the package on the bed where Mrs. Dorn had been lying. He opened the package, unfolding the items one by one. "We finally have something to do," he muttered, glancing quickly at Ellery, a hint of excitement in his eyes.
[574] Ellery bent over the desk, poking at the bundle with his pale fingers. "Oil on fire, the flame burns brighter!" He looked slyly at Dr. Jennings. The doctor didn't notice his gaze, as he was stretching his neck, watching the items on the table.
[575] "What are you mumbling?" the inspector asked, continuing to poke at the clothes.
[576] "Gray," Ellery replied cryptically.
[577] Chapter 10: The Proof
[578] They crowded around the bed, peering over each other's shoulders as the detective pulled items out of the package.
[579] Dr. Jenning stood up impatiently, half-squatting and half-standing for a moment before sitting back down in his chair. He couldn't resist standing up again, and finally curiosity got the better of him. He sneaked over to the bed, peering between the two detectives' shoulders to get a better look.
[580] "What is this?" Detective Quin asked, holding up a long, pristine white lab coat. "Hmm... a surgeon's coat, is it? Hmm?" His gray eyebrows suddenly furrowed, and he glanced over at Dr. Jenning with a mocking expression. "Is this yours, doctor?"
[581] "How should I know?" Dr. Jenning muttered, squeezing himself between the two detectives and running his fingers over the gown. Ellery grumbled, "Does it fit you?" The inspector draped the gown over Dr. Jenning, and it fell to his ankles. "It's not mine," Dr. Jenning insisted. "It's too long."
[582] The gown was wrinkled but clean. It was evident that it had been recently washed and ironed.
[583] "This isn't a new coat," Ellery observed. "Look at these frayed hems."
[584] "And here's the laundry tag…" The inspector suddenly tugged at the gown, his fingers pinching the neckline where there were two distinct puncture marks, indicating where a laundry label had been attached.
[585] The old man discarded the gown.
[586] He then produced a small item with two straps attached, resembling an apron, made of linen. Like the surgical robe, this item was clean but wrinkled. It was apparent that it had been recently used.
[587] "Anyone could have this," Jinni said defensively.
[588] This was an operating mask.
[589] Another item was a standard surgical cap, nothing out of the ordinary. It was not new and had not been soiled, but it was quite wrinkled. Ellery took the hat from his father, flipping it inside out several times. He carefully adjusted his spectacles, examining the part near the scalp closely and using his nails to carefully pick at the seams and hems of the hat.
[590] After a long pause, he shrugged and placed the hat back on the table, leaving only one remark: "This murderer is too lucky."
[591] "You mean – no hair?" Jenny asked.
[592] "Something like that. You're pretty sharp, Dr. Jenny…" Ellery leaned forward, examining the fourth item that Quin picked up. The old man held the item up to the light and examined it closely. It was a pair of well-pressed work pants.
[593] "Look at this! What is it?" the officer exclaimed, tossing the pants onto the table and pointing to the thigh area with his index finger. Two inches above the knees of both pant legs, there were two circular creases.
[594] Ellery was immediately excited. He carefully unfolded the creases with a silver pencil, but it was quickly stopped. They bent over to examine the inside of the creases closely. It turned out that these creases were sewn. They were ordinary white thread, with each stitch spaced quite far apart. There were similar creases near the bottom of the pants, close to the feet.
[595] "Obviously," Ellery murmured, "our amateur tailor was in a hurry. Look, this stitching could have been done in a very short time…"
[596] "Thomas!" The officer quickly stood up, looking around, calling out.
[597] Willie appeared immediately in front of the gurney.
[598] "Can you trace this stitching?"
[599] "Impossible."
[600] "Take a sample and try."
[601] Willie took out a pencil knife and cut about two inches of thread from the folds of his right pant leg. He carefully sealed this piece of thread into a glass evidence bag and stored it, as if he were collecting a hair from the criminal's head.
[602] "Doctor, try this pant on," the officer said sternly, "No, not really wear it, just show me how it fits."
[603] "And add these folds," Ellery thought aloud, "If all these folds were let out, the pant leg would be four inches longer... Doctor, what is your height?"
[604] "Five feet five inches," the doctor said, throwing the pants back to the officer.
[605] Ellery shrugged. "That doesn't prove anything," he said, "The original owner of this pant should be around five feet nine inches tall. But," he said with a cold smile, "this isn't even a good lead. This pant could have been stolen from any of the hundreds of hospitals in New York or from any of the thousands of doctors out there, or..."
[606] He stopped. Detective Quin was flipping through the surgical gown, mask, hat, and pants, revealing a pair of white canvas shoes - low-top Oxford shoes. The old man's hand was reaching forward...
[607] "Wait!" Ellery interrupted sharply. "Don't touch those shoes, Dad..."
[608] He stared at the shoes, lost in thought. "Lieutenant! Have you touched these shoes before?"
[609] "No, I just found this whole pile of stuff and brought it over as is. The shoes have been in this package the whole time. I didn't touch them."
[610] Ellery bent down and took out the silver pencil again. This time, he used the pencil to pick at the white shoelace of the right shoe, carefully observing.
[611] "Ah-ha – we have something here!" He straightened up, whispering to his father. The old man nodded skeptically.
[612] The shoelace was near the third pair of shoelace holes and had a small piece of rubber tape stuck to it, about half an inch wide, clean on the outside. There was an unusual indentation in the center of the rubber tape that caught the old detective's interest. He looked up, puzzled, at Ellery.
[613] "I'd bet a cookie that the shoelace broke," the old detective mumbled, "The indentation is because the ends of the shoelace broke, and there wasn't enough length in the middle, so it was taped together with the rubber tape."
[614] "You're not quite on the mark," Ellery whispered. "Rubber tape! That piece of rubber tape! It's amazing!"
[615] Dr. Jenning's eyes widened. "Bullshit!" He said sternly. "What's so amazing about that? It's just someone's shoelace broke, and they taped it up with rubber tape. I only noticed the shoe size issue, did you guys look at it? This shoe is clearly a size smaller than the one I'm wearing."
[616] "Maybe so. No, don't touch it!" Ellery exclaimed as Dr. Jenning reached for the shoe. The surgeon was startled by his shout, shrugged his shoulders, and looked around apologetically. He hobbled back to the far corner of the room and sat down, waiting silently. His eyes held a look of determination.
[617] Ellery lifted a corner of the tape with the tip of his pen and gently peeled off the inside of the adhesive tape with his index finger. "Sorry, doctor," he said, "I just wanted to perform a little unauthorized surgery. Willie, lend me your pencil knife."
[618] He separated the two ends of the tape, one of which had a serrated edge. He grabbed a corner of the tape and gently pulled, and the tape came loose. "It's still wet," he said excitedly, "Evidence - evidence! You noticed, Dad -" He quickly got up and went to Willie, "I need a plastic evidence bag, old friend! - This adhesive tape was hastily attached in a very hurried manner, and it hasn't been firmly fixed to the shoelace yet. And the adhesive is very strong." He carefully placed the adhesive tape into the evidence bag and put it in the inner pocket of his jacket.
[619] He leaned over the gurney again and carefully pulled out the upper half of the broken shoelace from the shoelace hole, then pulled out a portion of the lower half of the shoelace, trying to bring the two ends as close together as possible and tying them up. However, he found that if he did so, he would have to pull the shoelace out a few more inches to make a knot. And then, the length of the shoelace at the top would not be enough.
[620] "We don't need a wizard to see," he turned around and smiled at the detective, "that if the perpetrator had simply connected the two broken ends, the length of the shoelace at the top would not have been enough to make a knot. That's why he had to resort to using adhesive tape. We should thank the divine anonymous shoelace manufacturer."
[621] "But Elicker," the old police officer asked, puzzled, "what does this mean? I don't see anything to be happy about."
[622] "Trust me, sir, my excitement must have a reason," Elicker grinned. "Well, if you must know, I can explain. Suppose your shoelace could break at any time – if it happened to break at a particularly urgent moment – and you find that if you simply attach the two broken ends, the remaining shoelace would not be long enough to tie a knot. In such a situation, what would you do?"
[623] "Oh!" The old police officer tugged at his gray beard. "Well, I guess I would use something else to replace the shoelace, like the killer did. But even so –"
[624] "The suspense is killing me," Elicker said reproachfully.
[625] At this point, Detective Piggott coughed a few times, clearly trying to attract the attention of those present. The old police officer turned impatiently.
[626] "What is it?"
[627] Piggett's face was red with embarrassment. "I noticed something unusual," he said sheepishly. "Where did the shoe tongue go?"
[628] Ellery burst out laughing. Piggett's pride was hurt, and he looked at Ellery curiously. Ellery took off his glasses and began to clean them. "Piggett, you should win a raise for this," he said.
[629] "Eh? What?" The detective's face showed a vague displeasure. "Are you kidding me?"
[630] Ellery made a face. "Well, look here," he said. "After talking about the shoelace, this – we can call it the 'surprising mystery of the missing shoe tongue' – has become a major focus of this investigation. Where are they? I found – this."
[631] He picked up a shoe and felt around with his fingers under the shoelace, all the way to the tip of the shoe chamber. He fumbled around in the shoe chamber for a while and finally pulled out the shoe tongue that was hidden inside.
[632] "Look, it's here," Ellery said solemnly, "and it's clearly obvious that the shoe tongue was pressed and folded in, neatly pressed against the upper wall of the shoe chamber... and, unless the most likely small theory fails -"
[633] He grabbed the left shoe and dug around, and the shoe tongue in the same position was also the same.
[634] "This is really strange," Detective Quinn complained, "Litt, are you sure you didn't touch this shoe before?"
[635] "Johnson can vouch for me," Litt said sadly.
[636] Ellery's sharp gaze shifted from the old detective to Litte. He left the gurney and leaned down, lost in thought.
[637] "You really ought to take a good look at these shoes," he muttered, pacing around the surgical preparation room. "Doctor Jenning."
[638] The surgeon closed his eyes and answered, "Yes."
[639] "What size shoe do you wear?"
[640] Dr. Jenning glanced down at his own canvas shoes – the same as the ones on the table.
[641] "Looks like I'm lucky," he said slowly, suddenly standing up, popping up like a jack-in-the-box, "Still suspecting me, huh?" He glared at Ellery, his eyes wide, shouting, "Ha! You're wrong, Quin, my shoe size is six and a half!"
[642] "Yes, your feet are small," Ellery mused, "but look, these shoes are smaller, only six!"
[643] "Six," the old detective interjected, "but -"
[644] "Shh..." Ellery smiled, "I'm so pleased with the culprit's choice of footwear - it's nothing to do with you, Dr. Jenning. Where did you find this clothing, Little?"
[645] "On the floor of the phone booth at the southwest corner."
[646] "So…" Ellery pursed his lips and thought for a moment, "Doctor Quinn, did you see the rubber band I took off the shoe strap? Is it the same brand as this one?"
[647] "Of course. What about it? Every hospital in the city uses this brand."
[648] "I won't be disappointed by that news," Ellery said, "I don't expect to be that lucky… Oh, Doctor, none of these clothes are yours, are they?"
[649] Dr. Quinn spread his hands, "What good does it do to say no? You don't care what I say anyway. And I need to check my closet to confirm it."
[650] "Could the hat and mask be yours, perhaps?"
[651] "Could be anyone's!" Doctor Jenning adjusted the collar of his surgical gown. "You saw how long it's too long for me and the pants—what a poor disguise! And I'm sure those shoes aren't mine."
[652] "I'm not so sure," the old detective said challengingly, "There's no clear evidence these things aren't yours."
[653] "Oh, there is, Dad," Ellery said softly, "Look here."
[654] He flipped the shoes over and pointed to the soles for everyone to see. The shoes looked worn, with the heels worn down significantly and the soles smoothed out. The right heel was more worn on the right side, while the left heel was more worn on the left side. Ellery brought the two shoes together, allowing everyone to get a closer look.
[655] "Please look closely," Ellery said slowly, "The heels of both shoes, they're worn down almost equally..."
[656] The detective glanced at the floor where Dr. Jenning's left foot had been, and noticed that the doctor had been putting most of his weight on the other foot when he walked.
[657] Ellery continued, "Jenny doctor is right. These shoes are definitely not his!"
[658] Chapter 11: Interrogation
[659] Dr. John Minge's well-ordered mind was once again shaken by the morning of Abigail's death. His hospital was in chaos. Interns walked up and down the corridors, openly violating the hospital's code of conduct, smoking and loudly discussing the details of the murder case. The women seemed to have no need to follow the hospital's rules either, laughing and chattering until the angry nurses chased them back to their work stations and wards.
[660] The ground floor of the hospital was filled with various investigators and police officers. Minge frowned and stumbled through the crowded corridors, stopping at the door to the surgical preparation room. He knocked on the door, and a smoking detective opened it, letting him in.
[661] He quickly glanced around the room. Dr. Jennings, pale as a ghost, stood in the center, glaring. Inspector Quin looked confused and annoyed, standing across from him. Ellery Quin leaned against a gurney, twirling the white canvas shoes with his fingers. There were uniformed police officers scattered throughout the room, watching silently but alertly.
[662] Markham coughed, and the old detective turned on his heel, walking over to the desk. Dr. Jennings's face regained some color, and he slumped into a chair, looking like a deflated sack.
[663] Ellery smiled and greeted, "John, what's up?"
[664] "I'm sorry to bother you," Markham said, looking a bit tense, "there was a minor incident in the lounge, so I thought I should—"
[665] "Is it Miss Dunn?" Ellery asked immediately.
[666] "Yes. She's in a state of collapse. I think it would be best to get her home as soon as possible. Do you think so?"
[667] Ellery and his father exchanged a few words quietly. Inspector Quinn stood up, looking glumly at Ming-chin. "Dr. Ming-chin, do you really think that the young lady needs...?" He hesitated for a moment before mentioning another matter, "Who is her closest relative here?"
[668] "Mr. Downing - Hendrik Downing. He is her uncle - the only brother of Abigail Downing. I also suggest that a female companion be with her - perhaps Miss Fowler..."
[669] "Mrs. Downing's nurse?" Ellery said slowly. "No, she wouldn't be suitable. At least not at the moment... John, is Miss Downing and Miss Turing familiar with each other?"
[670] "They only know each other."
[671] "This is a bother, then." Ellery chewed on his fingernail. Dr. Meringue looked puzzled, not understanding what the "problem" referred to.
[672] Chief Inspector Quinlan interrupted impatiently. "Well, I say, son... Miss Dougherty shouldn't stay in the hospital now, if she feels terrible - poor thing - let her go home. Let her go now, and we can continue the investigation."
[673] "Okay," Ellery's furrowed brow remained unchanged, and he absent-mindedly patted Dr. Meringue's shoulder. "Call Miss Tennings to accompany Miss Dougherty and Mr. Dougherty. But before they leave... uh, it would be best, Johnson, for Mr. Dougherty and Miss Tennings to come to my office. I won't keep them long. John, I suppose there's a nurse with Miss Dougherty now?"
[674] "Of course. And young Mousses is with her as well."
[675] "What about Miss Fowler?" Ellery asked.
[676] "She's there too."

[959] [1] Refers to a wealthy, lecherous man who is generous to young women.
[960] [2] A Greek mythological creature with a human torso and a bull's head, trapped in the center of a labyrinth.
[961] [3] Three Roman consuls who formed the triumvirate of the Roman Triumvirate.
[962] [4] From the Hebrew Bible, meaning "stop, think for a moment." Often interpreted as "take a break, reflect."
[963] [5] Paracelsus (Philippus Aureolus Theophrastus Bombastus von Hohenheim, 1493-1541) was a famous Swiss physician and alchemist. A controversial figure, his alchemical research introduced lead, sulfur, iron, and arsenic into the field of pharmaceutical chemistry.
[964] Chapter Twelve: Experiments
[965] Dr. Plauti brushed the crumbs off his thigh and stood up, sticking his index finger into the open mouth of the sandwich and, seemingly oblivious to everyone around him, scraping the remnants of the sandwich. [967] Ellery Queen stepped back a few steps with a straight face as Morris Kensal walked into the operating room from the hallway.
[968] Inspector Quin glanced at Morris Kensal and immediately classified him as a "card character". The features he possessed, when considered separately, would not have been particularly surprising; but when seen together, they made him an unusual and bizarre figure. He was a short, dark-skinned man with a mop of unruly black beard. His eyes were deep and soft - like a woman's eyes, but not particularly remarkable. But under the magic of nature, these strange features combined to make Morris Kensal the most unusual character in the investigation of the Abigail Dowen murder case, the most unusual character the Quin family had ever encountered.
[969] His fingers were pale and stained from years of chemical exposure, with brown spots and burn marks. The tip of his left index finger was flattened, his lab coat was stained with various colors and had holes, and his sleeves and shoes were also corroded and worn.
[970] Ellery half-opened his eyes, thoughtfully closing the door and pointing to a chair.

He was not afraid, nor was he alert or evasive; he simply did not care about what was happening around him, nor did he feel anything.
[973] He sat alone in his own world, like a tiny figure in a science fiction novel, adrift in the vastness of space.

[978] "Hmm," Ellery crossed his legs and spoke in a casual tone, "I've long argued that science is another form of nihilism…you don't seem surprised by this tragic news, doctor."
[979] Dr. Kensel's gentle eyes widened with confusion and surprise. "My dear friend!" he protested, "For a scientist, death is not an emotional factor. I feel the unfortunate circumstances, naturally, but it doesn't reach the level of sentimentality. 'May the soul of the departed rest in peace' [1] and the like, so cliché. I'd rather quote a Spanish proverb—'She was a good woman, widely respected, and now she has passed away, returning to the dust.'"
[980] Ellery raised his eyebrows, his eyes sparkling with humor and anticipation.
[981] He eagerly said, "I bow to your erudition, Dr. Kessel. [982] "Allow me to add to the aphorism you quoted—'Waiting to wear the shoes of the departed, one is likely to end up barefoot.' And it's interesting," he continued, "that this proverb comes from Denmark."
[983] Kessel replied in a cheerful and serious tone, "And I remember, there's a similar saying in French. Many proverbs share the same root."

[985] The police officer chuckled beside him.
