[994] The old detective frowned, his fatherly emotions brewing into annoyance.
[995] Ellery smiled again. "Excellent, Dr. Kensel. Since you know exactly what I'm going to ask you, there's no need for me to ask. Please just answer me directly!"
[996] Dr. Kensel stroked his beard. "It's no problem for me, Mr. Queene - is that your name? I hope I got it right… You're obviously wondering what Dr. Jenning and I are working on, right?"
[997] "Absolutely correct."
[998] "Scientific systematic intelligence training is truly beneficial, you see!" Dr. Kensel commented humorously. They both smiled at each other, their voices filled with joy, as if two old friends who hadn't seen each other in years.
[999] Ellery replied gravely, "Dr. Kensel, your infinite insight seems to have a few unexplored areas - if I may add… I would like to know more details. I want to know what kind of alloy we are researching; I want to know how much money we have invested in this project; I want to know your background and what led you and Dr. Jenning to collaborate on this heroic scientific project; I also want to know why Mrs. Dorn decided to stop providing financial support for your project…" He paused, grinning, "And I also want to know who killed Mrs. Dorn, but I think…"
[1000] "Oh, those are not small problems, sir - not at all small," Dr. Kensel smiled slightly. "My scientific training has always taught me that to solve a particular problem, an excellent analyst must possess the following: first, painstakingly collect and organize all the phenomena; second, infinite patience; and third, to construct a comprehensive understanding of the entire problem with a new and unbiased imagination… However, what I just mentioned did not solve your problem.
[1001] "What kind of alloy are we researching? I apologize, but I must refuse to disclose any further information. First, knowing the details of the alloy won't help you uncover the crime. Second, our work with Dr. Jennings is strictly confidential… However, I can give you this description: once our work is complete and we are satisfied with the results, we can proudly declare that this alloy will make any type of steel obsolete in the world!"
[1002] There was silence in the room, and the district attorney and his assistant exchanged a quick glance before turning back to the bearded little scientist with renewed interest.
[1003] Ellery chuckled. "I'm not trying to pry, but if you can replace steel with a cheaper, more powerful metal, you and Dr. Jennings will become millionaires overnight."
[1004] "Absolutely. That's why the walls of the lab have been reinforced. They're now more secure, with new, state-of-the-art safes and security measures in place to prevent anyone from prying or stealing. I can tell you this much," Dr. Kessel said proudly, "our final product will be much lighter than steel, with better ductility and malleability, yet with the same strength as steel, and at a significantly lower cost of production."
[1005] "You didn't accidentally discover the Philosopher's Stone, did you?" Ellery asked solemnly.
[1006] Kensel's enigmatic gaze suddenly sharpened. "Do I look like a charlatan to you, Mr. Quine?" he asked directly, "Dr. Jenning's trust and support are the crucial guarantee and strong assurance of my scientific achievements.
[1007] "Let me tell you," his voice suddenly raised, "we have improved the future building material! It will cause an aviation revolution; it will solve one of the major problems that astrophysicists have been unable to solve for years - the incredible lightweight building material! The material's strength is not inferior to steel, and it can be used to manufacture space bridges, space elevators, and conquer the entire solar system. This alloy can also be used to manufacture various items, from safety pins, pens, to skyscrapers… And," he concluded, "it's all going to happen!"
[1008] There was a brief silence in the room. These words sounded incredibly arrogant, like a dream, but coming from the mouth of the small expert, they took on a certain air of becoming reality.
[1009] Ellery was not like the others, who were swept away by the emotion. He said, "I really don't want to put myself in the position of those who mocked and burned Galileo or mocked Pasteur, but as an analyst - I would like to know, to make it clearer, how much money has been spent so far, Dr. Kensel?"
[1010] "I'm not entirely sure, but I think it's probably over eighty thousand dollars. The financial aspect is mainly handled by Dr. Jenning."
[1011] "Just a small experiment," Ellery muttered to himself. "Not very big... Well, sir, chromium, nickel, aluminum, carbon, molybdenum - obviously, these minerals can't cost so much, unless you buy them by the truckload. No, Dr. Kensey, you need to explain more deeply."
[1012] Kensey cautiously smiled. "I think you should be familiar with the minerals used in the experiment. You will probably think of molybdenum, molybdenum lead, white tungsten, molybdenum oxide, and other minerals that can be refined to extract molybdenum. But I didn't say I was using molybdenum. My method of solving this problem is different from traditional scientific thinking...
[1013] "Speaking of expenses, you forgot a lot of infrastructure expenses. For example, the establishment of the entire laboratory, and the purchase of equipment. You know a special ventilation and exhaust system, a furnace, a complete set of refining equipment - turbines, electrolysis equipment, cathodes, and so on - how much do you think it costs?"
[1014] "I'm sorry, I'm a complete amateur. Can you tell me about your background, Dr. Kensey?"
[1015] "German University of Munich, University of Paris, Massachusetts Institute of Technology. I have been a special researcher at the Vienna Zubik Research Center and the Paris old Quartz Research Center. I worked at the American Bureau of Mines for three years and became an American citizen. After that, I worked in the largest steel group in the Americas for five years. In the process, I conducted exploratory research independently, and this alloy project began to germinate at that time."
[1016] "How did you and Dr. Jenny get to know each other?"
[1017] "We met through a fellow scientist. At that time, I was poor and needed someone to invest in my experiment to provide me with experimental funds and technical support, and more importantly, to find a trustworthy business partner... Dr. Jenny met these three conditions. He was very enthusiastic about this project. I won't explain any further, you can infer it."
[1018] Ellery shook his body lightly. "Why did Mrs. Dowen decide to stop investing in your project?"
[1019] Kessel's eyes wrinkled in the middle. "She just got tired of it. Two weeks ago, she called Dr. Jenny and me to her house. The experiment that was supposed to be completed in six months had been dragging on for two and a half years and still hadn't produced results. She said she had lost interest. Her tone was friendly, but her decision was unquestionable.
[1020] "We left her house in a dejected manner. Fortunately, we still had some money, so we decided to continue the research until we ran out of money. We didn't want to skimp on our lifestyle, so we continued the experiment in the same way. During this period, Dr. Jenny continued to try to find some investment from outside."
[1021] District Attorney Sampson cleared his throat and suddenly asked, "Did she explicitly tell you that a new will was being drafted by a lawyer?"
[1022] "Yes, she did."
[1023] Inspector Queene tapped the scientist's knee. "Do you know if the new will has been drafted and signed by her?"
[1024] Kessel shrugged. "I don't know. I sincerely hope she hasn't signed it. If the first will remains valid, things would be much easier."
[1025] Ellery said gently, "You don't care whether the second will has been signed or not?"
[1026] "I never allow worldly concerns to interfere with my work," Kensel calmly stroked his beard. "I'm a metallurgist and a philosopher. What will be, will be."
[1027] Ellery stretched and rose wearily from his chair. "You're a good man, too pure for this world, really," he said, running his hand through his hair and looking down at Kensel.
[1028] "Thank you, Mr. Quinn."
[1029] "But I still feel that you're not as calm and unaffected as you pretend to be, Dr. Kensel. For example!"
[1030] "Oh, on the contrary, Mr. Quinn," Kensel's dark face gleamed with white teeth. "I'm not surprised at all, as your methods and motives are too obvious. In fact, from a moral standpoint, I find your indirect defamation to be quite disproportionate to your intelligence… Are you finished, sir?"
[1031] Ellery suddenly stood up. "No, not yet. Do you know that Dr. Jennings will inherit a portion of Davenport's private property?"
[1032] "I am well aware."
[1033] "Well, you can go now."
[1034] Kessel nimbly rose from the chair, bowing deeply to Ellery in a European classical manner. He then nodded to the old policeman, the district attorney, Crooning, and Velli, and left the operating room with composure.
[1035] "Alas," Ellery groaned, sinking back into the empty chair, "even with divine favor, Ellery Quine must admit that he has encountered a formidable opponent here."
[1036] "Bullshit!" The old detective took a puff of his cigarette and jumped up. "That guy is just a human test tube."
[1037] "A cold fish," Sampson grumbled.
[1038] While questioning Kensington, the reporter Harper had remained silent, hunched over in a chair in the far corner of the operating room, his hat pulled low over his eyes. He had remained motionless throughout the process, but his gaze had never left the scientist's face.
[1039] Now he rose and paced around the room. Ellery looked up at him, and the two exchanged a silent glance.
[1040] "Well, old friend," Harper finally spoke up, "this time you've really got a hot potato on your hands. Don't mind me mixing my metaphors, but it's like a human-shaped iceberg's hot potato."
[1041] "I agree with you, Pete," Ellery smiled weakly, stretching out his limbs. "It's quite clear that you haven't forgotten a scientific fact, that is, that most of an iceberg is hidden underwater."

[1044] Chapter 13: Management
[1045] Chief Inspector Villey's large hand rested on the doorframe, and he was chatting heatedly with the unseen subordinate standing in the corridor.

[1111] Chapter 15: Complex

The mansion and garden faced Fifth Avenue, situated between two numbered streets in the sixties, occupying the entire block. [1269] In the history of the relationship between the police and reporters, no one has left a more interesting record than Peter Harper. While enjoying special treatment from the police, he never betrayed their trust. [1339] Chapter 17: The Enigma
[1340] The relationship between Inspector Quine and his son was particularly special - it was more like a friendship than a father-son bond - and it was especially evident during meal times. "Sara Fowler and Dr. Lucius Dunning, an unusual combination...a seer and a doctor, a non-typical pair." He suddenly smiled at his father, "A female prophet and a doctor, an intriguing combination."
[1363] "Indeed, quite interesting," the detective agreed. He wrapped his robe tighter around himself, "We'll have to get to the bottom of this tomorrow morning."
[1364] "It's clear," Ellery said with a sense of satisfaction, "As the Russian proverb goes, 'A man is wiser in the morning than in the evening.' Let's see what tomorrow brings."
[1365] The old man said nothing. Almost instantly, the cheerful expression on Ellery's face disappeared. He quickly stood up and went into the bedroom.
[1366] Chapter 18: Concentration
[1367] The news of Abigail Dove's murder spread like wildfire across the American news media, sweeping through the country and eventually reaching every corner of the globe the following day.
[1368] On Monday morning, every newspaper in the United States featured the story on its front page, with many offering only a meager amount of inside information. New York's media outlets, lacking substantial investigative material, had to fill in the gaps with detailed accounts of Abigail Dove's remarkable rise to fame, her unparalleled financial dealings, the intricacies of every charitable donation she made, and her long-ago affair with the late Charles van der Donk. One publishing house even rushed to produce a series of articles about her life, compiling them into a book titled "The Life of Abigail Dove."
[1369] In the afternoon's various editorials, the first rumblings of thunder could be heard. These editorials all subtly referenced New York City's Police Commissioner Quin, as well as the city's entire police force. One in particular had a distinct political tone, targeting the mayor. "Twenty-four precious hours have passed," it fumed, "yet not a single clue, not a single fact has been uncovered. The killer remains at large, his hands stained with the blood of a great woman, whose great and noble soul now cries out in the night, prematurely ascending to heaven."
[1370] "Can the fearsome Quin Commissioner, who has successfully apprehended countless ruthless criminals over the years, finally solve this major case?" one newspaper asked. Another directly claimed that the New York Police Department, as the world's largest city's manager, had been "infamous and inept" for years. Now it faced a century's worth of problems. This time, it would prove to the world just how incapable it truly was.
[1371] The only newspaper in New York that neither mocked nor criticized the police was the one with Peter Harper as its reporter, which puzzled citizens.
[1372] It was not necessary for the media to satirize and accuse the bureaucrats to awaken their stiffness and lack of vitality. The political and social worlds had already experienced a major earthquake, the intensity of which was recorded by the police. Prominent figures from various industries sent telegrams and phone calls, demanding that the mayor limit the time to solve the case. Wall Street was stirred up by the incident, and stock prices inevitably plummeted, with the entire stock market in a panic. The federal government showed an unusual interest in this case. One senator even gave a passionate speech in the House of Representatives because Abigail Dunn owned vast assets in his state.
[1373] The city hall was thrown into chaos by the flurry of heated meetings, and Central Avenue buzzed like a giant beehive. No one could find Inspector Quin; Chief Inspector Velli firmly refused to grant interviews to reporters. The rumors fueled by mystery and suspicion spread rapidly throughout the city. Privately, people whispered that the killer was a powerful financial magnate who was "protected" by the authorities, who strangled Abigail Dunn to avenge his humiliating defeat in a financial duel against the old lady years ago. This rumor sounded absurd, but it did not stop its spread in the city. Within two hours, the government had taken notice of it...
[1374] On Tuesday evening, a group of dignified officials secretly gathered in the most secretive sanctuary - the mayor's office. Sitting at the conference table, smoking was the mayor himself, with the police commissioner, District Attorney Sampson and his assistants, the Manhattan borough president, and five or six secretaries in order. Inspector Quin was absent from the meeting, which was particularly conspicuous.
[1375] The conference table was shrouded in oppressive clouds. They had discussed the case from various angles, but it was still a mess. The reporters surrounded the outer offices, hoping to gain the opportunity for an interview. The mayor held a thick stack of reports in his hand, which were signed by Inspector Quin and contained all the details of the investigation of this case. This report included every conversation with the parties involved, every detail, and every detailed background information of every relevant person. These were all the materials the police had about the case up to Tuesday morning. The borough president was convinced that the Irishman Mike Caddis must have somehow been involved in the murder case. He seemed to have been hired by Abigail Dunn's mysterious enemy. The borough president was satisfied with this conclusion. They also fiercely discussed why Dr. Jeni kept silent and the search for Schwarz,
[1376] The meeting seemed destined to end without result. There were no new discoveries, and they hadn't even agreed on what to do next. The police commissioner had a hotline phone next to him, and the phone rang non-stop throughout the meeting, bringing bad news, either failed investigations or the discovery of a few useless leads.

[1380] Report on Michael Caddisy's investigation
[1381] At 10:15 a.m. on Tuesday, after a medical examination, Cadex was found to be conscious and able to provide testimony related to the Dove case. The interrogation took place in room 328 of the Dutch Memorial Hospital. He was admitted to the hospital for an appendectomy and then transferred to the room. At that time, he was weak and suffering from severe pain.
[1382] Cadex claimed to know nothing about the murder. The interrogation began by verifying the testimony of Dr. Biers and Nurse Grace Oberman. Their testimony stated that on Monday morning, while Cadex was lying in the anesthesia room preparing for an appendectomy, an unknown person in a white robe passed by the anesthesia room and went to the surgery preparation room. Cadex confirmed that he saw a person wearing a white robe, surgical cap, and white mask enter the anesthesia room from the south corridor, quickly cross the room, and enter the surgery preparation room. He did not witness the person's departure - as he was soon rendered unconscious by the anesthesia. He claimed not to recognize the man's appearance, only remembering that he appeared to be limping, but could not confirm it. However, this was not significant, as Dr. Biers and Miss Oberman's testimony already proved this point.
[1383] Below is part of the report on Hendrik Dove. We had promised to provide him with personal safety protection, so we had someone guarding him. [1384] Conclusion: Cadex has a perfect alibi. At the time of the murder, he was under anesthesia. There is no evidence to suggest that little Willie, the bulldog, and the gecko Joe are involved in the case. This line of investigation is temporarily concluded.

He was simply fulfilling his obligations in accordance with the will and adhering to professional ethics and taking responsibility, etc.

[1437] Part Two: The Disappearing Cabinet
[1438] Have you ever seen a log jam? In the side of the Kiamichi Mountains, rushing through high-altitude forests, you may see this scene… A large amount of freshly cut timber is being carried downstream by the current, and at a whirlpool, one log gets stuck. All the logs begin to intertwine, none can pass, and they stop, stirring and breaking apart, quickly piling up into a logjam.
[1439] The lumberjacks begin to search for the log that caused the jam, the culprit that blocked all the logs from moving forward—or the "key log." They found it! They dragged, turned, and pulled it out, and it was swept away. Then, as if Merlin's wand had passed over it, the towering wall of logs collapsed, and the logs rushed down the stream one after another.
[1440] Investigating a complex case, my young friend, can sometimes be like facing a log jam. Our logs—our clues—hurry towards a destination. Suddenly, a clue gets stuck, and the rest of them stubbornly try to move forward, quickly piling up into a mountain.
[1441] The detective, playing the role of the lumberjack, found the "key log"! Ha! The stubborn clues were tamed, and they quickly lined up in the rapids, with a clear and open view, rushing towards the distant sawmill - the truth of the case.
[1442] - by Dr. Gustav Guttenberg

[1447] Chapter 19: Target
[1448] On Wednesday morning, Inspector Quin sat unusually at his desk in the police station. [1517] Chapter Twenty
[1518] "He finally came!"
[1519] Early Wednesday morning, the rumor that had leaked from the police station finally took effect. Just after 9:30, a thin man in black clothing emerged from Central Street. As he passed by the police station, he nervously checked the door numbers of the buildings, as if he didn't know where his destination was. When he reached the 137th building, he discreetly examined the ten-story building, finally confirming that the district attorney's office was indeed located there. He straightened his black coat collar and entered the yellow-brick building.
[1520] He was the elusive and mysterious Schwarz.
[1521] This news spread like lightning throughout every corner and crevice of Central Street. It was first whispered from the secretary's office of the District Attorney's office to the dilapidated brown building next door - the Criminal Court, and then quickly spread across the Sighing Bridge to the eerie cemetery. The gatekeeper of the cemetery, every detective and police officer on the street, and all the traffic policemen within four blocks received the news within five minutes. Schwarz had disappeared into the elevator of the 137th building, guarded by two police detectives, and entered the personal office of District Attorney Sampson.

[1647] Chapter 22: Enumeration
[1648] Hours passed, and the small group of silent figures remained seated in the deceased's office. The dimly lit room was filled with a bluish-gray haze. [1682] Chapter 23: Third Case?
[1683] Ellery Quine walked down the cursed corridor of the Dutch Memorial Hospital to the police station, his father's office. He was plagued with frustration and confusion that lingered in his mind.
[1684] He had expressed his annoyance, wanting to immediately return to the Quine residence on West 87th Street to bury his troubles in Marcel Proust's novels. [1794] [1] Alphonse Bertillon (1853-1914), a French police officer, introduced the use of comparative anthropometry into law enforcement and is considered the father of fingerprint identification and the founder of modern forensic science.
[1795] Chapter 24: Retrial
[1796] Wednesday passed, and with it, the most sensational murder case in New York City drew closer to becoming an unsolvable mystery.

[1957] Challenge the reader
[1958] Dear readers, as we reach this point in the story of The Dutch Shoe Mystery, in accordance with the rules established in my first detective novel from several years ago, I will once again challenge the reader… I solemnly promise to all readers that you now possess all the clues necessary to solve the murders of Abigail Dunn and Dr. Francis Jenning.
[1959] Based on the facts at hand, through rigorous logic and unassailable reasoning, it is not difficult for readers to identify the killer of Abigail Dunn and Dr. Francis Jenning. I deliberately say it is simple, but in reality, it is not. Reasoning is a process of discovery, and this process requires your keen and sustained thinking.
[1960] Moreover, what was taken from the operating room and what the author said to Harper over the phone in the previous chapter have no bearing on solving the case… Through logic, you can deduce what the item was and what was said over the phone.
[1961] To ensure fairness, I once again solemnly declare that I had already deduced the answer before going to the cabinet and calling Harper.
[1962] - Ellery Queen
[1963] Part Three: Found Documents
[1964] Every investigator who has spent a lifetime in the field of crime will find, upon reflection, that they have accumulated a wealth of items that make them appear to have a collecting habit... I know a detective whose room is filled with deadly weapons; another who collects fingerprints... My personal weakness is a fondness for collecting paper items - of all sizes, shapes, colors, and uses - the only commonality being that they have all played a crucial role in a case.
[1965] In my collection, you will find a fragment of yellow cardboard from the "Performer" case - it helped me determine that the Brazilian murderer, who killed nineteen people, went to Guinea. You will also find a half-burned cigar paper that led to the arrest of the eccentric Peter-Peter, a traitor from Martinique [1]. My collection includes a wealth of closed-case materials, all of which were cracked with the help of seemingly innocent paper items, such as a pawn ticket, a twenty-year-old insurance policy, a price tag from a cheap coat worn by a woman, a pack of cigarette paper - and one, which I dare say, is the most remarkable treasure in my collection.
[1966] When it was found, it appeared to be a thick, blank sheet of paper soaked with water, without any writing or printing. It was so wet that it was almost impossible to keep intact. Yet, this seemingly harmless piece of paper became an important clue that hanged the greatest pirate of the twentieth century. It was a label from an old whiskey bottle that chemical analysis showed had been soaked in seawater...
[1967] - Extract from Detective Notes

[1971] Chapter 27: Clarification
[1972] Philip Mousley, Esq., Friday, January
[1973] To Detective Richard Quinn, 87th Street, New York
[1974] Dear Detective Quinn:
[1975] I am writing this letter, actually, to make a special request to Mr. Ellery Quinn. This morning, I had a conversation with him on the phone.
[1976] Mr. Queen informed me that he has learned some of the deceased's private secrets that have not yet been discovered by the police. He learned this yesterday from John Mingsen at the Dutch Memorial Hospital.
[1977] Given that the secret has been revealed, I have no further reason to remain silent or evade. I would like to take this opportunity to provide a more in-depth explanation and clarification regarding the Tanning-Fuller matter.
[1978] Before I begin the explanation, I must humbly remind you that this morning, Mr. Ellery Queen made a promise over the phone. Mr. Queen promised that he would do his best to ensure that the secret of Helga Down's lineage is not revealed to the press and to keep it from being recorded in the police case files, if possible.
[1979] The documents that Mrs. Down's will stipulated to be destroyed were her personal diaries. My client kept a diary every day for a year before the incident and for five years afterward. The diary was kept under strict supervision. Mr. Ellery Queen has already wisely deduced that on Monday morning, I did not exercise my legal right to destroy the envelope, but instead violated the law and ethics by opening it and reading its contents.
[1980] Dear Inspector Quinn, as a seasoned lawyer, I am well aware of my loyalty to the law and my father's reputation. However, in this case, Mrs. Down was more like a relative to me than a client, and I must do my utmost to protect her interests. If Mrs. Down had died of natural causes, I would never have violated her trust and entrustment. But she was murdered, and I am - and remain - the fiancé of Miss Down, whom her deceased guardian had promised to me, so in fact, I am a member of the Down family - for all these reasons, I dared to open the envelope and read its contents as a family member rather than a lawyer. If I had turned it over to the police, the unrelated private secrets would have been made public. Therefore, I opened it privately as a family member, not as a lawyer, and immediately cautioned myself to report any facts related to the crime to the police if I found any.
[1981] However, after reading the diary, I discovered a terrible secret about Helga's background... Officer, please forgive me for hiding the secret and destroying the diary. It will have no effect on me, but please consider the poor young girl Helga. How would she face the world knowing she was the illegitimate child of the housekeeper?
[1982] I have one more thing to clarify... This can be referenced in the validated will. Helga is the heir to most of the Dunn estate, with no conditions attached to the inheritance, regardless of her origins or social status. In fact, Helga is the legitimate daughter of Abigail Dunn, and her true background will not affect her inheritance. Therefore, I have gone to great lengths to conceal this secret not out of selfishness, but because Helga's inheritance has nothing to do with blood ties, and I am not seeking personal gain.
[1983] Mr. Quinn's speculation about the cause of the argument between Abigail and Sarah Fuller is completely correct. They argued over the secret of Helga's background. The diary records Sarah's regret over the previous agreement and her repeated threats to reveal the truth to get her daughter back. However, as time passed, Abigail's love for Helga grew. She was very worried that Sarah would reveal the secret, so she kept her on, despite her age and mental state.
[1984] After Mrs. Dunn's death, I secretly spoke with Sarah Fuller and obtained her explicit promise. Once Abigail—her target of hatred—had passed away, I—for reasons unknown to me, she seemed to like me—would marry Helga, and she promised to keep the secret and never reveal it. Dr. Turing would surely keep quiet and keep the secret, as his career and reputation depend on his silence.
[1985] Mr. Ellery Quinn easily deduced that Sarah Fuller had met with Dr. Turing several times in recent days to discuss the matter of Helga's background. It is bizarre that, to this day, she bears him no ill will. She told me yesterday that they had discussed all the details and that Dr. Turing had agreed to never bother Helga again, to let her live independently, and to consider her as a legitimate member of the Dunn family.
[1986] The diary reveals another important fact: Dr. Jennings also knows this secret. Detective, you should already know that Dr. Jennings was a close friend of Mrs. Dove. He was one of the few people who knew the secret of Hilda's origin. The diary also records Dr. Jennings' attitude towards Dr. Tanning. Despite Dr. Tanning's grave mistake at the time, Dr. Jennings respected his scholarship and entrusted him with important tasks, and forgave him from the heart. However, Dr. Jennings reproached Sarah for her mental problems and her mother's instinct, which often caused trouble without considering Hilda's happiness. His attitude towards Dr. Tanning's kindness was related to his admiration for Dr. Tanning's professional competence. And after the incident, Dr. Tanning's diligent and hardworking work made him believe that Dr. Tanning had turned over a new leaf and was worthy of forgiveness.
[1987] The words in the diary indicate that Dr. Jennings was a close friend of Mrs. Dove. He defended her actions and found no evidence of any rift between them.
[1988] Once again, I reiterate that I am keeping silent not for personal gain. I believe you should understand this. Hilda is everything to me, and I cannot take any risks that may harm her.
[1989] Yours sincerely,
[1990] Philip Maurice
[1991] Postscript: I sincerely suggest that you destroy this letter immediately after reading it. This letter is printed only once and has no copies.
[1992] Detective Inspector Quinn can always remember that quiet Friday. Only one thing happened that day, and that was at 6:30 p.m., when Ellery Queen called.
[1993] Over the past 24 hours, Ellery's behavior has been subtly changing. He no longer seemed angry, no longer paced angrily around the room, and no longer appeared listless. For the past few days, he had been in that state.
[1994] All day Friday, he sat at the window in the living room reading. For two hours, he typed on the old, creaky typewriter. Detective Inspector Quinn rushed home for a hasty lunch during his midday break, and he saw Ellery typing a detective novel - a novel he had started months ago but had put aside a few weeks ago and never touched again.
[1995] The old man grunted, but a smile appeared under his gray beard. This was a good sign. For several months, he hadn't seen his son so relaxed and calm.
[1996] Detective Quinn returned home after a day of fruitless work, and the special phone call came in. The old man's face was etched with despair, but when he heard Ellery's voice in the bedroom answering the phone, his body tensed, and the wrinkles disappeared, and the gloom and despair vanished.
[1997] It was an excited voice – a fresh, joyful voice – a voice Ellery only used in unusual circumstances.
[1998] Detective Quinn quietly closed the outside door and stood there, holding his breath, listening carefully.
[1999] "Peter! Where are you?" The voice began with urgency, then sank, and then slowly became cheerful, "Incredible! Incredible, Peter! Connecticut, huh? Quite reasonable... quite troublesome? Hmm, that's okay... good job! Keep it safe. Got the certificate, did you? Great! ...Oh, wait, make a copy, and bring it to me when you get back to town – midnight, if necessary. I'll be waiting for you... Yes, go! Go!"
[2000] Detective Quinn heard Ellery hang up the phone, followed by Ellery's shout: "It's over, Juno!"
[2001] "What's over?" The old man asked as Ellery rushed into the living room.
[2002] "Oh, Dad!" Ellery grabbed his father's shoulders and gently shook him. "The case is closed. It's over, Pete Harper —"
[2003] "Pete Harper, huh?" The officer's face was stern, his lips expressing dissatisfaction. "If you need help, why didn't you call my people?"
[2004] "Dad," Ellery smiled, pulling the old man to the armchair, "you don't need to ask. This case isn't over yet. I can't let official people help me confirm a theory, and Pete is more suitable for this. Otherwise, if there's anything we missed, we'll have to explain a lot of things... Fortunately, it's over. Pete will bring an interesting file tonight... Please be patient, sir."
[2005] "Thank you for your help," the old man said, relieved. "I'll make sure to take good care of it.”
[2006] "Okay, son," the old man looked tired. He lay on the chair, closed his eyes, "I should rest…" But his wise eyes soon opened again, "Just twenty-four hours ago, you were completely clueless about these two cases and had no hope at all."
[2007] Ellery raised his arm and jokingly bowed in the air. "But at that time, I hadn't succeeded!" He called, "And today, I did. Because—allow me to quote Disraeli [1]—'Success is the child of daring'—and in reasoning, I was very daring. My good dad, you wouldn't believe it… From now on, I will follow the Gallic code: 'Charge ahead!'"
[2008] * * *
[2009][1] Benjamin Disraeli (1804-1881) was a British Conservative leader who served twice as Prime Minister of the United Kingdom and was also a novelist.
[2010] Chapter 28: Argument
[2011] Whenever a case reaches a climax, an atmosphere unavoidably permeates the apartment of the Quinn family. It is a sensation that pervades the air – a quiet excitement that they neither consciously control nor attempt to conceal. This feeling is subtly revealed in Juno's lively movements, and silently conveyed in the officer's irritable temperament and Ellery's brimming confidence.
[2012] Ellery invited his father's closest friends to a secret meeting held at his home. His plans remained shrouded in mystery. Although he had discussed his ideas with his father on Friday night, they did not intend to reveal any secrets. The unexpected visit of Peter Harper at 2:30 am on Saturday was also not to be mentioned. Perhaps the old detective was unaware of the details of the reporter's visit, as he was sound asleep in bed, wearing a robe and slippers, when Ellery opened the door and let Harper into the apartment. He offered Harper a strong whiskey and a few cigarettes, took a long, thin file from his hand, and sent him on his way. Ellery then returned to his room, his eyes dull and calm, to go to sleep.
[2013] At 2:00 pm on Saturday afternoon, Detective Quin and Ellery Quin warmly welcomed the arrival of two friends - District Attorney Sampson and Sheriff Willie. Juana was busy bustling around inside and out.
[2014] Sampson's gaze fell on Ellery. "This wind is blowing hard, it seems something is about to happen."
[2015] "It's a real whirlwind, you know," Ellery smiled. "Please have some coffee, respected prosecutor. We're about to embark on a fascinating journey of discovery."
[2016] "You mean – the case is closed?" Sampson asked skeptically.

[2018] "Of course." The chief inspector threw a stack of papers onto the desk. Ellery half-closed his eyes and scanned them. He then threw the files back onto the desk. "Well, that's not important now."
[2019] He sat back in his chair, settling into the most comfortable position – with his neck resting on the back of the chair. He gazed dreamily at the ceiling. "It was a thrilling pursuit," he mumbled. "It contained some truly magnificent elements – utterly magnificent. I can't remember the last time I was so happy – of course, I mean after a formal case resolution."
[2020] He grinned.
[2021] "I won't give you the straight answer right away... my reasoning is a bit complex, and I'd like to hear from Dad, or you, Sampson, and you, Chief Inspector Velli."
[2022] "Let's review the first case, shall we? There were two crucial pieces of evidence in the Abigail Down case. These two pieces of evidence seemed so innocent - just a pair of white canvas shoes and a pair of white overalls, that's all."
[2023] "What good are these pieces of evidence?" Sampson complained, "They're interesting, I'll give you that, but if you want to use them to convict, it's probably -"
[2024] "What good are these pieces of evidence?" Ellery closed his eyes completely, "After I present the following details, let's take another look at these two items and see what good they might do."
[2025] "We found a pair of shoes. These shoes had three distinct features: the shoelaces were broken, the shoelaces were taped together with rubber tape, and the shoe tongue was rolled up at the top of the shoe cavity."
[2026] "At first glance, the explanation is quite ordinary. The broken shoelaces are due to an accident, the rubber tape is used to stick and connect the two ends of the shoelaces. And what does the rolled-up shoe tongue mean?"
[2027] Samson's eyebrows wrinkled into two small mountains, and the giant Willie looked bewildered. The policeman listened attentively. The three of them said nothing.
[2028] "Can't you answer that?" Ellery sighed, "Well, let's explain this point by point. I must say that it was because the imposter's shoes had these three characteristics that I found the initial and most important path to the end of the case."
[2029] "This," Willie rasped, "Mr. Quine, are you telling me that you already knew who did this at that time?"
[2030] "Innocent Willie," Ellery smiled, "I never said that. But I did say that by analyzing the shoes and considering the related characteristics of the pants, my suspicion circle greatly narrowed. It's amazing, based on these two pieces of evidence, I can describe many characteristics of the criminal."
[2031] "For example," he continued, "take the pants. You must have noticed that the stitching on the upper part of the knees is quite interesting and provides a lot of information..."
[2032] "This indicates," the officer impatiently explained, "that the original owner of the pants is taller than the imposter. This guy stole the pants to make sure his shorter legs could fit into them. I don't see what else the pants can tell us."
[2033] Sampson's head shaking the cigarette vigorously. "I must be the biggest idiot in the world," he said, "I can't figure out what you're trying to say."
[2034] "That's too bad," Ellery grumbled, "May God have mercy on you. Let me continue with the second murder case. How did our dear doctor suddenly lose his life?"
[2035] "Here, allow me to make a hasty conclusion. There is only one thing that is hard to accept before some unexpected events occur. That is - the condition of the body of Jenna when it was found."
[2036] "Condition?" Sampson was puzzled.
[2037] "Yes. The facial expression of Genie after death is the simplest and most direct evidence. You all must remember that he was killed while working – writing the manuscript of 'Innate Allergies'. His face was so serene, as if in a dream; there was no surprise, no panic, no fear of death. The calmness of his face after death.
[2038] "Combined with the blunt force trauma at the back of his head and the posture of his body after death – you will find that this is a very interesting situation.
[2039] "When the second piece of evidence appeared in front of me, this situation became even more interesting."
[2040] "I don't find it interesting at all," Sampson said. He looked bored and uninterested.
[2041] "Relax, sir," Ellery continued, "the second clue... ah, the second clue! This is fate, gentlemen. Dr. Mignon moved the filing cabinet containing the case files that Jenning had collected. It was like a ray of light that shone down on my mind, instantly solving the case. It's so beautiful, so elegant! Dr. Mignon's awareness of property ownership was so strong that it almost caused me to miss this most crucial clue...
[2042] "If the second murder hadn't occurred, the real killer of Mrs. Dove might have gone free. I must admit, if Jenning hadn't been killed, I would have been at a loss today. Only after solving the case of Jenning's murder could I follow the clues and solve the case of Mrs. Dove's murder."
[2043] Inspector Quinns extended his finger into the snuff box. "I'm sorry, but my thoughts are exactly the same as my old friend Henry's," he said. "As usual, you explain the answer but don't explain the process, leaving me feeling like the kind of person who hears a joke but doesn't know the punchline, yet still feels obligated to laugh... ah, what about the filing cabinet? According to you, this evidence is just as important as the shoes, although I can't see the point in both. How did the filing cabinet solve the case?"
[2044] Ellery chuckled. "I just said that we're about to embark on a discovery journey. It's time to go. I must admit, in this situation, my pulse is beating differently from the normal state. And I promise you, gentlemen, I will arrange a big surprise for you... bring your belongings, gentlemen, and I'll call the hospital first."
[2045] They shook their heads and looked at each other. Ellery went into the bedroom. They heard him call the hospital.

Bring Jenna's doctor's filing cabinet back to his office and put it back in its original position... Understood?... Yes, go now. I'll be bringing several important people closely related to the case to your sacred place. Goodbye!"

[2103] Chapter 30: The Answer
[2104] Leading headlines in evening newspapers across the country reported the sensational news: Lillian Price, the dedicated nurse and secretary of the late Dr. Francis Jeni, was accused of murdering her employer, the renowned Abigail Dunn. She had been arrested.
[2105] The press spared no words. No further details were revealed.
[2106] Editors of major newspapers posed the same question to their crime reporters: "Is this accurate? Is it another scheme like the arrest of Schwarz?"
[2107] All except one answered, "I don't know."
[2108] Peter Harper was the exception. He barged into the editor's office, shut the door behind him, and engaged in a half-hour-long confidential conversation. He talked non-stop.
[2109] After he left, the editor picked up the thick stack of typed pages on the desk and began reading. His eyes bulged out, and he immediately made several phone calls, issuing instructions.
[2110] Harper had secured an exclusive from Ellery Queen, the only reporter in the news industry privy to the full details of the case. He knew that in a short time, as the industrial age printing presses rolled on, his exclusive would be reproduced in countless copies and disseminated worldwide. He hailed a taxi and sped towards the police station.
[2111] After working for Ellery Queen for thirty-six hours, he finally harvested the precious golden fruit.
[2112] The district attorney's office was in chaos.
[2113] After an emergency meeting with his assistant Timothy Crowning, District Attorney Sampson quietly slipped out of the office, avoiding a group of shouting reporters, and hurried towards the police station.
[2114] The city hall was in chaos at that moment. The mayor locked himself and a group of secretaries in his office. He paced back and forth in the room like a tiger - dictating records, issuing orders, and answering phone calls from all levels of the city's departments. Sweat dripped down his flushed face in large drops.
[2115] "Long-distance call, the governor is calling."
[2116] "Answer it!" The mayor snatched up the phone on his desk. "Hello, hello, Governor Sir…" In that instant, his voice suddenly became calm, and he put on a good face of a Washington, D.C. spokesman, confidently addressing the million citizens. "Yes, it's all over…confirmed. It was that woman, Price…I understand, Governor Sir, I understand…She was hardly mentioned in this case. She's one of the most cunning people I've ever encountered…Five days – that's a pretty fast pace, isn't it? – within five days, we've cracked two of the most sensational murder cases in the city's history!…I'll call you back later to report the details…Thank you, Governor Sir!"
[2117] He hung up the phone, and the humble expression on his face vanished instantly, large beads of sweat dripping down again. He continued to shout orders, disregarding his dignity. "Damn it! Where's the police commissioner? Call his office again! How the hell did they crack this case? Am I the only one in New York City who doesn't know what's going on?"
[2118] "Yes, Mayor Sir…I'm sorry I couldn't call you sooner. We interrogated him right after we caught him. We were busy – absolutely swamped. Ha-ha, ha-ha!…No, I can't report the details yet. But everything is under control. Don't worry…That woman named Price hasn't confessed yet. She's been silent…Yes, only temporarily. She's just being hopeful. She has no idea how much we know…Yes, Detective Queen has promised me that she'll confess today. We've got her cornered…What?…Of course! This case is extraordinary! Some details are truly surprising…Yes, yes, ha-ha! Goodbye."
[2119] The Commissioner of the New York City Police Department hung up the phone and slumped into the chair like a bag of flour.
[2120] "Damn it!" He whispered to his assistant, "Detective Queen could have given me a hint. I'm completely in the dark."
[2121] Two minutes later, he entered the corridor, frowning and with his eyes flashing, quietly making his way to Inspector Quin's office.
[2122] On this day, Inspector Quin's office was the quietest place in New York City. The old detective sat comfortably in his chair, issuing orders calmly over the intercom and occasionally dictating information to the stenographer, organizing files.
[2123] Ellery lay lazily on the long chair by the window, eating an apple. He smiled calmly and seemed at ease.
[2124] Junna sat on the floor next to Ellery, busy devouring a rectangular chocolate bar.
[2125] Detectives came in and out of the office door.
[2126] At this time, a plainclothes policeman stumbled in. "Hilda Dowen wants to see you, sir, shall I let her in?"
[2127] The inspector leaned back in his chair. "Hilda Dowen? Well, Bill, don't leave just yet. We'll only talk for a little while, and then you can take her out."
[2128] The detective left the office and soon brought Hilda Dowen in. The girl was dressed in black mourning clothes - a tall, striking girl with a flush of excitement on her cheeks. She grabbed the inspector's sleeve and her fingers trembled.
[2129] "Inspector Queequeg!"
[2130] "Please sit down, Miss Dowen," the inspector said kindly. "I'm glad to see you're in good health... What can I do for you?"
[2131] Her lips trembled. "I mean... I mean..." She faltered.
[2132] The officer smiled. "I guess you've heard the news?"
[2133] "Oh, yes! I think it's... just terrible." She had a bright, sweet voice typical of a girl. "It's so good that you caught this... this terrible, dangerous woman."
[2134] "She's guilty, Miss Dow. So, what else do you want to talk about?"
[2135] "Oh, I don't know where to start," she fumbled in her pocket. "This is about Philip, Philip Muhlhaus, my fiancé."
[2136] "What about your fiancé, Philip Morrow?" the policeman asked gently.
[2137] Her big, round eyes widened as she looked pleadingly at the policeman. "I'm so worried - that day you warned Philip. Remember, Chief Inspector Quin? - he destroyed those files. Now, you're not going to punish him, are you? The real killer's already -"
[2138] "I see," the old policeman patted the girl's hand. "If that's what's bothering your pretty little head, then forget it, dear. Mr. Morrow's behavior - we can call it poor judgment, and I was angry at the time, but now, it's all over."
[2139] "Oh, thank you so much!" Her face was radiant with joy.
[2140] Suddenly the door opened, and Bill, the policeman, was pushed into the office, stumbling forward. Philip Morrow rushed in after him, his eyes scanning the room. When he saw Helga Davenport, he rushed over to her, putting a hand on her shoulder, and glaring at the policeman. "Helga, they told me you were here - what are they going to do to you?"
[2141] "Ah, Philip!" She rose from her chair, and his hand tightened on her wrist. The two of them stared into each other's eyes, and then, suddenly, they both smiled. The officer frowned, Ellery sighed, and Juno's mouth hung open in surprise.
[2142] "I'm sorry —" The officer broke the silence. "Bill, you can go. Can't you see that this young lady is being taken care of by her prince charming?" The detective, sensing the cue, withdrew from the office. "So now, Miss Dunn — Mr. Moffat — although we're all very happy to see the two of you so happy, I must remind you that this is a police station…"
[2143] Fifteen minutes later, the officer's office had undergone a complete transformation.
[2144] Chairs were moved around the desk, forming a circle. In attendance were the district attorney, Sampson, the police commissioner, and Peter Harper. Juno sat behind the commissioner's chair, smoothing the coat that hung on the back.
[2145] Ellery stood by the window, whispering to Dr. Munding, "I imagine the hospital is in quite a state now, don't you think, John?"
[2146] "This is too terrible!" Dr. Margin exclaimed, "Nobody knows what to do or what to say. Everything is in disarray… It's Lucille Price! — How could this happen? It's just too unbelievable."
[2147] "Ah, the unexpected killer has remarkable psychological defenses," Ellery whispered, "La Rochefoucauld's aphorism: 'Innocence cannot protect itself as well as evil can—' It's a universal truth indeed. By the way, what was our distinguished metallurgist friend Kensal's reaction to this news?"
[2148] The doctor made a face. "You can imagine. The guy is not human. Now he has funding for his experiments, but he showed no signs of happiness or sadness over his partner's death. He just locked himself in the lab, immersing himself in his experiments, as if nothing had happened and with no emotional response whatsoever. He's like… like a cold-blooded snake."
[2149] "At least not a snake lurking in the grass," Ellery chuckled. "Still, I'd wager that Kensal is relieved that his theory was proven wrong. I'm also curious if his alloy theory is as brilliant as he thought it was… By the way, I didn't know snakes were cold-blooded until you mentioned it. Thank you for the information!"
[2150] "Here are the words I want you to record," Ellery said after a while, as Dr. Margin took his seat and the officer relinquished control of the situation to him. "Firstly, I must say that, since I began to understand and participate in my father's cases, I have never seen a murder case as meticulously planned as the Abigail Dohwn case."
[2151] "I really don't know where to start... I believe you have been puzzled by an incredible question for a long time - how could Lucille Price have become two different people at the same time? Many witnesses can confirm that she was indeed in the operating room during the preparation. Dr. Bayers, Nurse Grace Oberman, and the notorious 'Big Mac' suspect can all testify - but at the same time, these witnesses also confirm that they saw a man with behavior similar to Dr. Jeni's enter the operating room. That's the problem, how could Lucille Price have become two completely different people at the same time and appear on the scene?"
[2152] Everyone nodded vigorously.
[2153] "But this really happened, and I will explain in detail," Ellery continued. "How did she perform this grand illusion? I will analyze the whole process for you."
[2154] "Everyone, recall the situation at the time. The highly praised nurse Lucille Price was on duty, taking care of the unconscious Abby Down in the operating room. But at the same time, she disguised herself as Dr. Jeni with male features. Two absolutely reliable witnesses - a doctor and a nurse - swore that there were two people in the operating room at the time. [2258] "Hey, I was just thinking," Pete said thoughtfully, "how could the killer possibly plan such a complex murder plot within two hours after Abigail's accidental fall? [2259] "Great questions, Pete," Ellery seemed very happy. "I have wonderful answers for both of them.
[2260] "Mrs. Davenport had planned to undergo appendectomy a month later; everyone in the hospital knew this. Undoubtedly, the murder was also planned to take place at that time, but the method would have been different depending on the situation. For example, the operating room might have had an anesthesiologist because the elderly woman might have been conscious at that time. If the anesthesiologist was present, then Lucy Price would not have been able to carry out her murder plan before the surgery. I suspect she intended to harm Mrs. Davenport in the hospital room after the surgery, just as she did in the operating room. She would have also disguised herself as Dr. Jenny, just as she did this time. I am certain that she would have been assigned to care for Mrs. Davenport, as Dr. Jenny trusted her so much. Therefore, the basic details of the crime had already been planned and arranged before the unfortunate stairfall incident – the clothes were secretly hidden somewhere in the hospital, and the plan to take Jenny out of sight had already been discussed. There were many other details that were ready. Therefore, when the unfortunate incident occurred, only minor modifications were needed to the original plan. Moreover, the situation turned out to be even better than she could have hoped for – first, there was no anesthesiologist, which allowed the murder plan to be carried out directly. Then, she only needed to inform Schwartz of the situation, and the plan would be complete."
[2261] Ellery took another sip of water. "My throat is parched... As for your point that the murder was unnecessary, that doesn't hold water: because Mr. Mignot and Dr. Jenning were both confident that Mrs. Donovan would pull through. Lucille Price was always by their side, so she would have known their confidence and certainty. What if Mrs. Donovan recovered? The appendectomy would be postponed indefinitely, and Lucille would have to wait indefinitely for her plan to fail. No, Pete, the accident only accelerated the murder; it was not the cause of the murder."
